Valuation of a Mudabbar: Two-Thirds of a Qinn
Explains valuation of mudabbar via benefit analysis and intra-madhhab difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for the value of a mudabbar, explain the reasoning based on the three benefits of a slave, and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether it is two-thirds or one-half.

Core Idea
A mudabbar's value is two-thirds of a qinn because tadbir removes one of three core benefits: saleability.

In Hanafi fiqh, the valuation of a mudabbar (a slave declared free upon the master's death) differs from an ordinary slave (qinn). This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah uses a benefit-based analysis to arrive at the rule, and then reveals that there is disagreement within the school. Understanding this reasoning is essential for correct calculations in inheritance and transactions involving mudabbars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three benefits of a slave
List the three categories of benefit (manafi‘) that a master derives from a slave: intercourse (for females), service, and profit through sale.
The passage also mentions an alternative list: service, profit through sale, and paying debts after death.
2
Understand how tadbir eliminates the benefit of sale
When a master declares tadbir, the slave cannot be sold. Therefore the benefit of sale (or profit through sale) is lost completely.
Intercourse and service are retained because they do not contradict the tadbir status.
3
Derive the two-thirds valuation
Since one of three benefits is lost, the value reduces proportionally: the mudabbar retains two of the three benefits, so its value is two-thirds of a qinn.
This logic assumes the three benefits are of equal value. The passage treats them as equal for valuation.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and the mufta bihi
The preferred view (al-ashahh) is two-thirds. However, a second opinion holds it is one-half, reasoning that before tadbir one benefits from the slave itself (via sale) and from its substitute (via hiring), and after tadbir only the latter remains. Some later scholars said the fatwa is on the half view.
The mufta bihi in general practice remains the two-thirds view from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن المنافع ثلاثة أنواع : ١. الوطء والاستخدام والبيع فبالتدبير فات البيع ، ولا يُضَمِنُ المدير المعتق الثلث الذي ضَمَّنَهُ السَّاكِتُ مع أن ذلك الثلث صارَ ملكا للمدبِّر بسبب الضمان ثلاثة : [ ١ ] قوله : لأن المنافع ...الخ ؛ حاصله : إنّ المنافع التي تُؤخذ من المملوك أنواع أحدها : الوطء. وثانيها : الاستخدام ؛ أي جعله خادماً. وثالثها : البيع، يعني الاسترباح بواسطة البيع ونحوه ، بالتدبير تفوتُ منفعةُ البيع ، ويبقى النوعان الآخران، فلذلك تنقص قيمة المدبّر عن قيمة القن بقدر الثلاثة، وتكون قيمة المدبر ثلثي قيمة القن، ثمّ هذا التقرير لا يجري إلا في الأنثى ؛ لأنّ الوطء لا يوجد إلا فيها، فالأولى ما في «الكافي وغيره : إنّ منافع المملوك الاستخدام والاسترباح بالبيع، وقضاء الديون بعد موته، وبالتدبير يفوتُ الاسترباح، ويبقى الآخران، فتكون قيمة المدبّر ثلثي قيمة القن. وذكر في «العناية» وغيرها : إنّ هذا - أي كون قيمة المدبر ثلثي قيمة القن - أحد الأقوال، وهو الأصح ، والقولُ الثاني : إنَّ قيمة المدبّر نصف قيمة القن؛ لأنَّ قبل التدبير ينتفع بعينه بالبيع ونحوه ، وببدله بالإجارة ونحوه، وبالتدبير يفوت الأول ويبقى الثاني ، وقيل : عليه الفتوى.
Because benefits are three types: 1. intercourse, 2. service, 3. sale. By tadbir the benefit of sale ceases, and the mudabbar does not guarantee the third that the silent partner made him liable for, even though that third became the mudabbar’s property due to the guarantee. Three: [1] His statement: 'because benefits...' etc. Summary: The benefits taken from a slave are types; one of them is intercourse, second is service, i.e., making him a servant, third is sale, meaning profit through sale and the like. By tadbir, the benefit of sale is lost, and the other two remain. Therefore, the value of a mudabbar decreases from the value of a qinn to the extent of the third, and the value of a mudabbar becomes two-thirds of the value of a qinn. Then this explanation applies only to females because intercourse exists only in them. The better view is in al-Kafi and others: The benefits of a slave are service, profit through sale, and paying debts after his death. By tadbir, profit is lost and the other two remain, so the value of a mudabbar is two-thirds of the value of a qinn. It is mentioned in al-Inayah and others that this — that the value of a mudabbar is two-thirds of the value of a qinn — is one of the opinions, and it is the most correct. The second opinion is that the value of a mudabbar is half the value of a qinn because before tadbir one benefits from the slave himself through sale and the like, and from his substitute through hiring and the like; by tadbir the first is lost and the second remains. It is said that the fatwa is upon this.
Source pages p. 630

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling passage from the commentary of Al-Wiqayah, explaining why the value of a mudabbar slave is two-thirds that of an ordinary slave (qinn). The author begins by stating that the benefits derived from a slave fall into three categories: sexual intercourse (only applicable to female slaves), service, and the ability to profit by selling the slave. When a master declares tadbir (suspensive emancipation upon death), the benefit of sale ceases because a mudabbar cannot be sold. However, the benefits of intercourse and service remain. Consequently, the value of a mudabbar decreases by one-third compared to a qinn, leaving it at two-thirds. The author notes that this reasoning applies only to female slaves since intercourse is exclusive to them. He then cites a superior explanation from al-Kafi and other works, which replaces intercourse with paying debts after death and keeps service and profit through sale. Under this explanation, the value is also two-thirds. The author then mentions that in al-Inayah and other sources, three opinions are recorded: the first (two-thirds) is the most correct; the second (one-half) is based on the idea that before tadbir one benefits from the slave itself via sale and from its substitute via hiring, and after tadbir only the latter remains; the third opinion (unspecified) is said to be the one given in fatwa. This passage demonstrates Hanafi talil (legal reasoning) by analyzing the effective cause of value reduction.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whom the master has declared free upon the master's death (tadbir). The mudabbar cannot be sold but may be used for service and intercourse.
قن (qinn): An ordinary, unrestricted slave who can be sold, hired out, and used for all purposes.
منافع (manāfi‘): Benefits or usufructs derived from a slave, here enumerated as intercourse (for females), service, and profit through sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred opinion in this passage, why is the value of a mudabbar two-thirds that of a qinn?
A) Because tadbir removes the benefit of service, leaving only intercourse and sale.
B) Because tadbir removes the benefit of sale, leaving two of the three benefits.
C) Because tadbir removes all three benefits, leaving no value.
D) Because tadbir doubles the value due to the hope of freedom.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi valuation of a mudabbar is two-thirds of a qinn based on the loss of the benefit of sale, though an alternative opinion values it at half.

Indemnity Calculation in Co-owned Mudabbar Case
Illustrates indemnity rules in co-owned mudabbar case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate indemnity for a mudabbar in a three-partner co-ownership and explain the distribution of wala' according to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A mudabbar’s ownership through indemnity is ascription-based (mulk mustanid) — it does not create retroactive liability.

This lesson unpacks a detailed case from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah: three co-owners of a slave, one declares tadbir, another frees his share, and the third remains silent. We will walk through the indemnity calculations, the subtle concept of 'ascription-based ownership' (mulk mustanid), and how the wala' (patronage) is divided. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn, which we will compare.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the slave's value
There are three co-owners: one declares tadbir for his share, one frees his share, and one remains silent. The slave's total value is 27 dirhams. Determine the value of each share (1/3 each = 9 dirhams).
The value is given as 27 dirhams for easy calculation.
2
Compute indemnity from mudabbar to silent partner
The silent partner's share is 9 dirhams. Since the mudabbar's tadbir is valid only for his share, the silent partner can claim indemnity from the mudabbar for the full value of his share because the mudabbar's tadbir potentially spoils the silent partner's property. Thus the mudabbar pays 9 dirhams to the silent partner.
This indemnity is for the loss of the silent partner's share due to the tadbir.
3
Compute indemnity from freeing partner to mudabbar
The mudabbar's share as a mudabbar is valued at two-thirds of the total (since tadbir reduces the value): 18 dirhams. The freeing partner's act of freeing spoils that mudabbar share. The mudabbar already paid 9 dirhams to the silent partner, so his net loss is 18 - 9 = 9 dirhams? Actually the text says the mudabbar takes 6 dirhams from the freeing partner. The calculation: the mudabbar's share as mudabbar is 18. He pays 9 to silent, leaving his net interest at 9. The freeing partner's freeing destroys that 9, so he owes 9? But the text says 6. This may be a textual error. The main point is that the mudabbar does not owe additional indemnity for the share he acquired through indemnity payment.
The exact numbers are less important than the legal principle.
4
Understand mulk mustanid (ascription-based ownership)
The mudabbar owns the silent partner's share only after paying indemnity, but this ownership is ascribed back to the time of tadbir for some purposes (like wala'), but not for liability. Because it was not owned before the freeing, the freeing partner's act did not spoil a share already owned by the mudabbar at that time, so no additional indemnity.
This is a key Hanafi concept: ownership can be effective for some purposes but not others.
5
Determine wala' distribution
Abu Hanifa: The mudabbar gets two-thirds of the wala' (his original share plus the one he bought) and the freeing partner gets one-third. The Sahibayn: All wala' goes to the mudabbar because tadbir of a part is tadbir of the whole.
The difference stems from how they view the effect of tadbir on the whole slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] قوله : ولا يضمن.... الخ ؛ توضيحه : أنه لو كان العبد قيمته سبعة وعشرين درهماً فدبّر أحد الشركاء الثلاثة سهمه، وعتق الثاني، فالساكت يأخذ من الذي دبَّر ثلث قيمة الكل، وهو : تسعة دراهم، والمدبّر يأخذ من المعتق ستة ؛ لأنَّ قيمة المدبّر ثلثا قيمة القن، فلما كانت قيمة القنّ سبعة وعشرين كانت قيمته مدبّراً ثمانية عشر درهماً وثلث ثمانية عشر سنّة ، فيأخذ المعتق ذلك القدر لكونه إنّما أفسد نصيب المدبر حال كونه مدبّراً.  لأنه ملكه بأداء الضمان ملكاً مستنداً ، وهو ثابت من وجه دون وجه ، فلا يظهرُ في حق التضمين، وأما الولاء للمدبر فثلثاه للمدبر وثلثه للمعتق (وقالا : ضَمِنَ مدبره لشريكيه كان موسراً، أو معسراً) ولا يأخذ المدبّر من المعتق تلك التسعة التي أدّاها إلى الساكت بناءً على أَنه لَمَّا أدّى ضمان نصيب الساكت لملكه بعوض التسعة، فكان إعتاق المعتق إفساداً لسهمي المدبّر : أحدهما : سهمها وثانيهما: سهمه الذي ملكه بأداء الضمان إلى الساكت ، فينبغي أن يأخذ ضمان ذلك السهم أيضاً، وإنما لا يضمن لأنَّ ملكه في سهم الساكت إنما ثبت بأداء الضمان لا قبله ، فكان ثابتاً من وجه دون وجه ، فلا يثبت بمثله الضمان. [١] قوله : ملكاً مستنداً ؛ يعني أن ملكه ثبت عند أداء الضمان مستنداً إلى ما قبل الإعتاق، فهو من حيث ثبوتِهِ عند أداء الضمان لم يكن ثابتاً قبله، ومن حيث استناده إلى سبب وجوبه ، وهو التدبير كان ثابتاً قبل الضمان من حين التدبير، فكان ثابتاً من وجه دون وجه فيكون معتبرا في حق الضمان والمضمون له دون غيرهما ; لأنَّ الثابت بالضرورة يتقدّر بقدرها. كذا في «الكفاية». [٢] قوله : للمدبّر ; لأنَّ أحد الثلثين كان له أصالة والآخر تملكه بأداء الضمان للساكت، فصار كأنه دبّر ثلثيه من الابتداء، فيكون له ثلثا الولاء، بخلاف المعتق ; فإنّه وإن كان له ثلث أعتقه وثلث أدّى ضمانه إلى المدبّر لكنّه لم يمتلك ما أدى ضمانه ; لأنه قد سبقه التدبير، فلم يبق محلاً للنقل من ملك إلى ملك. فضمانه ضمان إفساد لا ضمان تملك ومعاوضة، فلا يكون له إلا ثلث الولاء، ولو كان الساكت اختار سعاية العبد فالولاء بين الثلاثة أثلاثاً، هذا كله على رأي أبي حنيفة لله له ، وأما عندهما فالولاء كله للمدبّر ; لأنَّ تدبير البعض تدبير لكلّه. كذا في «الفتح»
[2] His statement: 'and he does not guarantee...' etc. Explanation: If the slave was worth twenty-seven dirhams, and one of three co-owners declared tadbir for his share, and the second set him free, then the silent one takes from the one who declared tadbir one-third of the total value, which is nine dirhams. The mudabbar takes from the one who freed six dirhams, because the value of a mudabbar is two-thirds of the value of a qinn. Since the value of a qinn is twenty-seven, its value as a mudabbar is eighteen dirhams, and one-third of eighteen... so the one who freed takes that amount because he spoiled the mudabbar's share while it was mudabbar. [page 631] Because he [the mudabbar] owns it [the share acquired] through payment of indemnity in an ascription-based ownership, which is established from one aspect but not from another, so it does not appear in the context of liability. As for the wala' for the mudabbar, two-thirds of it belong to the mudabbar and one-third to the one who freed. (And they both said: The mudabbar is liable to his two co-owners whether he is wealthy or poor.) And the mudabbar does not take from the one who freed the nine dirhams that he paid to the silent one, on the basis that when he paid the indemnity for the silent one's share, he owned it in exchange for nine dirhams. So the freeing by the one who freed corrupted both shares of the mudabbar: one is his original share, and the second is the share he owned through payment of indemnity to the silent one. Thus he should take indemnity for that share as well. But he does not become liable because his ownership of the silent one's share was only established by the payment of indemnity, not before. So it was established from one aspect and not from another, and therefore liability is not established by the like of it. [1] His statement: 'ascription-based ownership' means that his ownership was established at the time of paying indemnity, ascribed to the time before the freeing. So from the perspective of its being established at the time of payment, it was not established before, and from the perspective of its ascription to the cause of its necessity (i.e., tadbir), it was established before the indemnity from the time of tadbir. Thus it is established from one aspect and not from another. Therefore it is considered valid in the context of liability and the person liable, but not in other contexts, because what is established necessarily is measured by its necessity. This is as in al-Kifayah. [2] His statement: 'for the mudabbar' – because one of the two-thirds was originally his, and the other he owned through payment of indemnity to the silent one, so it is as if he had declared tadbir for two-thirds from the beginning. Thus he gets two-thirds of the wala'. Unlike the one who freed, for although he had one-third that he freed and one-third for which he paid indemnity to the mudabbar, he did not own what he paid indemnity for because tadbir preceded it, leaving no room for transfer from ownership to ownership. So his indemnity is an indemnity of spoiling, not an indemnity of ownership and exchange. Therefore he gets only one-third of the wala'. And if the silent one chose the slave's earning, the wala' is divided equally among the three. All this is according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (the two Sahibayn), all of the wala' belongs to the mudabbar, because tadbir of a part is tadbir of the whole. This is as in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 630

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling illustration from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah, explaining the case of a slave co-owned by three partners. One co-owner declares tadbir for his share, another frees his own share outright, and the third remains silent. The silent partner takes an indemnity of one-third of the total value (9 dirhams) from the one who declared tadbir. The mudabbar then takes from the freeing partner 6 dirhams, calculated as his mudabbar share’s value (two-thirds of 27 = 18, minus the 9 he already paid to the silent one? Actually the calculation: the mudabbar’s share as a mudabbar is worth 18, and since he paid 9 to the silent one, he remains with 9 value in the slave. The freeing partner’s freeing ruined that 9-value? The text says the mudabbar takes 6 from the freeing partner, but it is not fully clear; the main point is that the mudabbar does not have to pay additional indemnity for the share he acquired through indemnity payment. The reason given is that the mudabbar’s ownership over the share he bought from the silent partner is 'ascription-based ownership' (ملك مستند): it is established at the time of payment but ascribed back to the time of tadbir for certain purposes only. Because it was not actually owned before the payment of indemnity, he does not become liable for spoiling that share if the freeing partner later frees the slave. On the issue of wala' (patronage), Abu Hanifa rules that the mudabbar gets two-thirds (his original share plus the one he bought) and the freeing partner gets one-third. The two Sahibayn differ, holding that tadbir of a part is tadbir of the whole, so all wala' goes to the mudabbar. This passage demonstrates Hanafi rules of tadbir, indemnity, and wala', with the subtle concept of mulk mustanid.

Key Terms
ملك مستند (mulk mustanid): Ownership that is legally established at a later time but ascribed back to an earlier cause, giving it partial effect (here for liability purposes).
ولاء (walā’): The bond of patronage that arises between the emancipator and the freed slave, entitling the former to inherit from the latter under certain conditions.
سعاية (su‘āyah): The earning of the slave to pay off his value when the slave is partially freed or when the master cannot afford to free him fully.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case described, why does the mudabbar not take indemnity from the freeing partner for the share he acquired through payment to the silent partner?
A) Because the mudabbar’s ownership of that share was established only at the time of payment, not before the freeing, so it is not considered complete for liability.
B) Because the freeing partner did not spoil that share; it remained intact.
C) Because the silent partner already paid for that share.
D) Because the mudabbar did not pay the indemnity to the silent partner.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the mudabbar’s ownership of a share acquired through indemnity is ascription-based and does not trigger additional liability for spoilage.

Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn on Divisibility of Tadbir
Explains the contradictory rulings on tadbir by a co-owner based on divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa and the Two Sahibayn differ on the divisibility of tadbir and state the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) for the liability of the mudabbar.

Core Idea
Tadbir's divisibility determines whether a co-owner's declaration affects only his share or the whole slave.

This passage from a hashia on Al-Wiqayah clarifies the foundational dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two illustrious students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding the nature of tadbir. At issue is whether the legal act of tadbir can be divisible (tajazzu') when applied by a co-owner to his own share only. This seemingly technical question has direct practical consequences for the liability of the mudabbar and the rights of other co-owners.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the core legal question: whether tadbir declared by a co-owner on his share is divisible or indivisible.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds tadbir is divisible; it applies only to his share but spoils the shares of others.
3
State Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn hold tadbir is indivisible; it applies to the entire slave.
4
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical difference: liability of the mudabbar and indemnity for co-owners.
5
Note the mufta bihi
Identify the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
Abu Hanifa's view is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] قوله : وقالا...الخ ; الفرق بين مذهبه ومذهبيهما أنَّ التدبير لَمَّا كان متجزئاً عنده قصر تدبيره على سهمه، لكنّه فسد به نصيبُ الآخرين، وعندهما صار الكل
[3] His statement: 'And they both said...' etc. The difference between his opinion and the opinion of the two of them is that since tadbir is divisible according to him (Abu Hanifa), he restricts his tadbir to his own share, yet it spoils the shares of the others. According to them (Sahibayn), the whole becomes...
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from the super-commentary (hashiya) on Al-Wiqayah, clarifying the fundamental difference between Abu Hanifa and the Two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) regarding the nature of tadbir (suspensive emancipation). According to Abu Hanifa, tadbir is divisible (mutajazzi’). That is, when one co-owner declares tadbir for his share only, it applies only to his share and does not extend to the entire slave. However, the act of tadbir spoils the other co-owners’ shares because the slave cannot be sold or used as collateral, causing loss. The silent partner can demand indemnity. In contrast, the Sahibayn hold that tadbir is indivisible; when one co-owner declares tadbir, it applies to the entire slave, making the whole mudabbar. Consequently, all co-owners are affected, and the mudabbar becomes liable to all of them for the slave’s value, regardless of his financial status. This difference has practical implications for indemnity (whether the mudabbar is liable to all or only to some) and for wala' distribution. The text here is cut off at 'صار الكل', but the full explanation is found in the referenced works like Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
تدبير البعض (tadbīr al-ba‘ḍ): The declaration of tadbir by a co-owner limited to his own share in a jointly-owned slave.
تجزؤ (tajazzu’): Divisibility/partial applicability of a legal act, here tadbir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the fundamental disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding tadbir of a share in a co-owned slave?
A) Whether the mudabbar can be sold.
B) Whether tadbir is divisible (applies only to the share) or indivisible (applies to the whole slave).
C) Whether the silent partner can demand indemnity.
D) Whether the mudabbar is liable if he is poor.

Key Takeaway
The divisibility (tajazzu') of tadbir is the root of the intra-Hanafi dispute, affecting the scope of emancipation and co-owner compensation.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Liability and Wala of a Co-owned Mudabbar
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mudabbar's liability and wala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a mudabbar's guarantee of co-owners' shares and the distribution of wala, and identify the key legal principle underlying the difference.

Core Idea
Ownership established from one aspect does not generate full liability; tadbir's divisibility is the pivot.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about a mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is deferred to his master's death) who is co-owned. When one co-owner emancipates his share, the mudabbar must compensate the other co-owners. The disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hinges on whether the mudabbar's ownership of the share he acquires through compensation is fully established, and whether tadbir is divisible.

Guided Path
1
Set the scenario
Understand the basic facts: a slave is owned by three partners. One partner declares tadbir on his share (making the entire slave mudabbar? or only his share?). Another partner (the mu'tiq) immediately emancipates his share. The mudabbar then pays the third (silent) partner for his share, acquiring ownership of that share retroactively.
The mudabbar's payment to the silent partner is a 'dhaman tamalluk' (guarantee for ownership).
2
State the Sahibayn's position
Recall the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: the mudabbar must guarantee both partners, whether he is wealthy or poor. The mu'tiq's emancipation does not reduce the mudabbar's liability. All wala (clientage) goes to the mudabbar because tadbir of a part is tadbir of the whole.
Sahibayn treat the entire slave as mudabbar, so the mu'tiq's act is invalid or irrelevant.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that the mudabbar guarantees only his original share (his own person as mudabbar?). He does not have to guarantee the share he acquired from the silent partner. The wala is split: two-thirds to the mudabbar, one-third to the mu'tiq.
Abu Hanifa argues that the acquired share is owned only 'min wajh duna wajh' (from one aspect, not another).
4
Analyse the key concept: thabit min wajh duna wajh
The commentary explains that the mudabbar's ownership of the silent partner's share is established at the moment of payment but is retroactive to before the emancipation. However, because it was not fully present before the payment, it is considered partial. This partial ownership cannot generate a full guarantee.
This concept is derived from the principle 'al-thabit bi al-darurah yataqaddaru bi qadriha' (what is established by necessity is limited by its extent).
5
Determine thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the mu'tiq gets one-third of the wala and the mudabbar's liability is limited. Under the Sahibayn, the mudabbar bears full liability for both shares and gets all wala.
Later Hanafi fatwa tends to follow the Sahibayn's view for simplicity.

Source Text

«الفتح»
Book of Emancipation, Chapter on Partial Emancipation. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: His mudabbar guarantees his two partners, whether he is wealthy or poor. Because he owns it through payment of the guarantee, as an ownership that is retroactive (mustanid), and it is established from one aspect and not another, so it does not appear in the matter of liability. As for the wala, two-thirds of it belong to the mudabbar and one-third to the one who emancipates (the mu'tiq). (And the two said: His mudabbar guarantees his two partners, whether he is wealthy or poor.) And the mudabbar does not take from the mu'tiq those nine dinars that he paid to the silent partner, based on the fact that when he paid the guarantee for the silent partner's share, he owned it in exchange for the nine dinars. So the mu'tiq's emancipation was an invalidation of the mudabbar's two shares: one is his original share, and the second is the share he owned by paying the guarantee to the silent partner. Thus, he should take the guarantee for that share too, but he does not guarantee because his ownership in the silent partner's share is established only by the payment of the guarantee, not before, so it is established from one aspect and not another, and therefore guarantee is not established by it. [1] His saying: 'ownership that is retroactive' means that his ownership is established at the time of paying the guarantee, retroactively to before the emancipation. So from the perspective of its establishment at the time of payment, it did not exist before; from the perspective of its retroaction to the cause of its obligation (which is tadbir), it existed before the guarantee from the time of tadbir. Thus it is established from one aspect and not another, so it is considered valid for the purpose of guarantee and the one guaranteed for, but not for others, because what is established by necessity is limited by its extent. Thus in al-Kifaya. [2] His saying: 'for the mudabbar' means that one of the two-thirds was originally his, and the other he owned by paying the guarantee to the silent partner, so it is as if he had made tadbir for two-thirds from the beginning, so he gets two-thirds of the wala. This is contrary to the mu'tiq, for even though he has one-third which he emancipated and one-third for which he paid the guarantee to the mudabbar, he did not own what he paid the guarantee for, because the tadbir preceded him, so it did not remain a subject for transfer from one ownership to another. So his guarantee is a guarantee of nullification, not a guarantee of ownership and exchange; thus he gets only one-third of the wala. And if the silent partner chose the slave's earning, then the wala is divided among the three in thirds. All this is according to the opinion of Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him), but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), all the wala belongs to the mudabbar, because tadbir of a part is tadbir of the whole. Thus in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a mudabbar (a slave whose emancipation is deferred to the master's death) in a case of shared ownership. The matn states the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn): the mudabbar must guarantee the shares of his co-owners regardless of whether he is wealthy or poor. The commentary explains the reasoning of Abu Hanifa, who differs. According to Abu Hanifa, when the mudabbar pays the guarantee for the silent partner's share, he acquires ownership of that share retroactively from the time of the tadbir, but this ownership is only from one aspect—it is established at the moment of payment but retroacted to before the emancipation. Because it is not fully established before payment, the mudabbar is not liable for the share he acquired by guarantee if the mu'tiq (the one who emancipates) later emancipates it. However, the mudabbar is liable for his original share because his ownership there is absolute. Consequently, the wala (clientage) is distributed: two-thirds to the mudabbar (his original share and the one he acquired) and one-third to the mu'tiq. If the silent partner chooses the slave's earning (sa'ayah), then the wala is split equally among the three. The Sahibayn hold that tadbir of a part is tadbir of the whole, so the entire slave is mudabbar, and the mu'tiq's emancipation is invalid, giving all wala to the mudabbar. The text clarifies that the dispute hinges on whether tadbir is divisible (tajazzu')—Abu Hanifa says it is, the Sahibayn say it is not.

Key Terms
مدبّر (mudabbar): A slave whose master has declared that he will be free after the master's death (tadbir). The text discusses a mudabbar who is co-owned and guarantees his co-owners' shares.
ولاء (wala): Clientage, the legal relationship that transfers inheritance rights to the emancipator. In this context, it is distributed among the owners of the slave based on their shares.
ضمان (dhaman): Guarantee or liability to pay compensation. Here, the mudabbar must pay his co-owners for their shares, either as a guarantee of ownership (tamalluk) or as a guarantee of damages (jinayah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa in this passage, why does the mudabbar not have to guarantee the share he acquired by paying the silent partner?
A) Because the silent partner waived the guarantee
B) Because his ownership of that share is established from one aspect only, not fully
C) Because the mudabbar is poor
D) Because the mu'tiq already paid compensation

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on tadbir's divisibility affects both the mudabbar's liability and the distribution of wala, with later preference for the Sahibayn's indivisible view.

Acknowledging a Shared Slave as Partner's Umm Walad: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on umm walad acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions regarding the acknowledgment of a shared slave as a partner's umm walad and distinguish the rulings of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment of another's umm walad binds the admitter but does not prejudice the denier's rights; the Sahibayn offer an alternative via istis'a'.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute that arises when one co-owner of a slave woman declares she is the umm walad of his partner (who denies it). The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents the positions of Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). Understanding this ruling illustrates the principles of acknowledgment (iqrar), shared ownership, and the concept of guarantee of ownership (daman tamalluk).

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
One co-owner (the muqir) acknowledges the slave is the partner's umm walad; the partner (the munkir) denies this. Determine what each party's legal position implies for ownership and service rights.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and its basis
Abu Hanifa holds that the muqir is bound by his acknowledgment, so he forfeits his rights in the slave. The munkir retains his half-share, thus she serves him one day and is idle the next. Understand the logic: the muqir admitted no right for himself; the munkir's claim is only for half.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling and its basis
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow the munkir to demand istis'a' (work for ransom) for half the slave's value, after which she becomes free. This is analogous to the mudabbar case where the guarantee is a guarantee of ownership (daman tamalluk), not conditional on wealth.
4
Compare the two positions: thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the slave remains partially in service; under the Sahibayn, she can be freed through istis'a'. The practical difference depends on whether the munkir prefers continued service or financial compensation and emancipation.
5
Examine the analogy to istilad and tadbir
The text compares umm walad acknowledgment to the case of istilad (claiming paternity from a shared slave) and tadbir (promise of emancipation), showing consistent principles of guarantee of ownership.
The guarantee of istilad is wajib regardless of wealth; the same applies to the guarantee of tadbir in the Sahibayn's view.

And if he says: 'She is the umm walad of my partner,' and the partner denies it, she serves him one day and is suspended one day. This is according to Abu Hanifa, because the one who made the acknowledgment (muqir) declared that he has no right over her, so he is held to his acknowledgment. Then the denier (munkir) claims that she is as she was (i.e., a shared slave), so he has no right over her except in half of her. As for according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the denier may demand that the slave girl work for ransom (istis'a') for half her value, then she becomes free. [In the mudabbar case, the following applies:] The mudabbar is obliged to pay the guarantee to his two partners, and the emancipation by the mu'tiq is not executed because it conflicts with the property of another, so the wala all belongs to the mudabbar, and his guarantee does not differ whether he is wealthy or not. And according to him (Abu Hanifa), the guarantee is only incumbent if he is wealthy, as previously mentioned. [1] His saying: 'because it is a guarantee of ownership' means that the guarantee of tadbir is a guarantee of ownership, because he owns her earnings, service, and sexual intercourse. So it is like the guarantee of istilad: if a slave girl is shared between two, and she gives birth and one of them claims the child, his lineage from him is established, and he guarantees her value to his partner. This does not differ based on wealth or poverty; it is obligatory even on the poor, unlike the guarantee of emancipation, for it is not a guarantee of ownership but a guarantee of injury. [2] His saying: 'and the partner denies' means his partner. So if he (the partner) confirms him, then she is his umm walad, and he must pay half her value and half her 'uqr (dower for intercourse), like the case of a shared slave girl who gives birth and one claims the child. Likewise if the claimant proves it by evidence. Thus in al-Bahr. [3] His saying: 'and she is suspended' means she does not serve either of the two partners on that day. As for the denier, his right to her service is only in one day, not every day. As for the one who acknowledged 'she is my partner's umm walad', he declared that he has no right over her, so he is held to his acknowledgment even if the other denies. [4] His saying: 'then the denier' means that the denier claims she is a shared slave as before, so he has his right in half of her as before; therefore we ruled she serves him one day. The one who acknowledged voided his right in half of her by his acknowledgment of the umm walad status for another, so we ruled she does not serve him. [0] His saying: 'So for the denier... etc.'; the gist of their (the two's) position is that the denier may demand that the slave girl work for ransom for...
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage presents another intra-Hanafi dispute about a shared slave woman. One co-owner says 'She is the umm walad of my partner' (i.e., the partner's childbearing slave). The partner denies this. According to Abu Hanifa, the acknowledging owner is bound by his admission: he forfeits his rights in her, so she serves the denier one day and is idle the next (suspended). The denier only gets his halved share of service because he claims she is still a shared slave. According to the Sahibayn, the denier can demand that the slave work to ransom herself (istis'a') for half her value, after which she becomes free. This is analogous to the mudabbar case: the guarantee here is a 'guarantee of ownership' (dhaman tamalluk), which is not conditional on wealth, unlike the guarantee of emancipation (dhaman i'taq), which is a guarantee of injury and depends on the guarantor's solvency. The commentary explains that if the partner confirms the claim, then she is indeed his umm walad, and he must pay half her value and half her 'uqr (a fixed compensation for intercourse). The text compares this to the case of istilad (claiming paternity of a child born to a shared slave woman). The ruling is based on the principle that an admission is binding on the admitter, but not on the denier. The passage ends with a summary of the Sahibayn's view that the denier can demand istis'a'.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has given birth to her master's child, thereby gaining the right to freedom upon the master's death. Here, one co-owner claims she is the partner's umm walad.
استسعاء (istis'a'): A legal device where a slave works to earn money to pay a ransom for freedom. Here, the Sahibayn allow the denier to demand istis'a' for half her value.
عقر ('uqr): A fixed compensation paid by a man who has intercourse with a slave woman believing her to be free or having no right; here, half the 'uqr is due if the claim is confirmed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa in this passage, what happens to the shared slave woman after one co-owner acknowledges she is the partner's umm walad?
A) She becomes free immediately
B) She serves the denier one day and is suspended one day
C) She serves both co-owners equally each day
D) She is sold and the proceeds are divided

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute on shared slave ownership, Abu Hanifa rules for partial service, while the Sahibayn allow the denier to seek istis'a' to free the slave, reflecting different applications of the guarantee of ownership principle.

Guarantee for Freeing a Shared Umm Walad by a Wealthy Owner
Intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee for joint umm walad freeing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee for freeing a jointly owned umm walad and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between Abū Ḥanīfa and the Ṣāḥibayn.

Core Idea
Abū Ḥanīfa requires guarantee; the two require the slave to work – appraisability is the hinge.

This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āyah presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement about the financial consequences when one co-owner frees a jointly owned umm walad (a slave mother of her master's child). The ruling hinges on whether the umm walad is considered appraisable (mutaqawwim) during the master's lifetime. Abū Ḥanīfa holds that she is, so the freeing partner must pay a guarantee; the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) hold she is not, so she must work for the other partner instead. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the scenario
We have two partners who jointly own an umm walad. One partner frees his share. Note that the freeing partner is wealthy (mūsir), which is the condition for the dispute.
If the freeing partner were insolvent, there would be agreement that no guarantee is due.
2
State Abū Ḥanīfa's position and its basis
Abū Ḥanīfa says the freeing partner must guarantee (ḍamān) the other partner's share. He reasons that the umm walad is appraisable because the partial ownership established through the child (juz'iyyah) is present in life, even if its effect on ownership is suspended for benefit.
The cause (sabab) of appraisability is present but its operation is postponed to after death.
3
State the Ṣāḥibayn's position and its basis
Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad say no guarantee is required. They argue that the umm walad is not appraisable because the necessity of benefit (ḍarūrat al-intifāʿ) suspends the effect of ownership, so she is like a mudabbar (slave promised freedom after death).
They analogize her to a mudabbar, where appraisability is dropped entirely.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: Under Abū Ḥanīfa's view, the freeing partner pays cash. Under the Ṣāḥibayn's view, the slave woman must work (saʿāyah) for the other partner to compensate for the lost share.
This distinction matters when the partner cannot pay or when the slave's labor is insufficient.
5
Determine the muftā bihi (operative opinion)
The passage does not specify which view is relied upon. Later Hanafi works often prefer Abū Ḥanīfa's view, but this is not settled. A mufti must know the tarjīḥ from later sources.
This is an unresolved tarjīḥ within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا حتى لو كانت أم ولد مشتركة بين شريكين أعتقها أحدهما ، وهو موسر يضمن عند أبي حنيفة ، وعندهما لا يضمن... متحقق في الحال، وهو الجزئية الثابتة بواسطة الولد على ما عرف في حرمة المصاهرة، إلا أنه لم يظهر عمله في حق الملك ضرورة الانتفاع ، فعمل السبب في إسقاط التقوّم، وفي المدبّر ينعقد السبب بعد الموت.
[١] قوله : شريكين ؛ بأن ولدت الأمة ولداً ، فادّعاه كل من الشريكين، فصارت مشتركة بينهما.
[٢] قوله : أعتقها أحدهما ؛ أي عتق أحد الشريكين نصيبه منها، فيعتق كلّها، أو عتق أم الولد لا يتجزأ عنده أيضاً، ولا يجب الضمان على المعتق ولا السعاية على الأمة عنده ؛ لعدم تقومها. كذا في «الخانية».
[٣] قوله : وهو موسر ؛ قيّد به لأنه محل الخلاف ، أما إذا كان معسراً فلا ضمان عليه اتفاقاً، بل تسعى عندهما بقدر نصف قيمتها.
No, even if there was a shared umm walad between two partners, one of them freed her, and he is wealthy, he guarantees according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two he does not guarantee... realized immediately, which is the partial ownership established through the child as known in the prohibition of affinity, but its effect did not appear with respect to ownership due to the necessity of benefit, so the cause operated to drop appraisability, and in the case of a mudabbar the cause becomes effective after death.
[1] His saying 'two partners': meaning the slave woman gave birth to a child, and each of the two partners claimed it, so she became jointly owned between them.
[2] His saying 'one of them freed her': meaning one of the partners freed his share of her, so all of her becomes free, for freeing an umm walad cannot be partial according to him as well, and neither is guarantee obligatory upon the one who frees nor is earning (sa'yah) upon the slave woman according to him, due to her lack of appraisability. Thus in al-Khaniyyah.
[3] His saying 'and he is wealthy': it is conditioned because it is the point of disagreement; as for when he is insolvent, there is no guarantee by agreement; rather she works for wages (tus'ā) according to the two to the extent of half her value.
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the guarantee (ḍamān) when one co-owner frees a jointly owned umm walad (mother of a child) while being wealthy. The core ruling is that according to Abū Ḥanīfa, the freeing partner must guarantee the other partner's share because the umm walad is appraisable (mutaqawwim) due to her partial ownership through the child. However, according to the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad), she is not appraisable because her value is negated by the child's connection, so no guarantee is due; instead, the slave woman must work to compensate the other owner. The marginal notes clarify: (1) the scenario of joint ownership arises when each partner claims the child; (2) freeing one share frees all, per the rule that umm walad cannot be partially owned; (3) the dispute is limited to the wealthy case—if the freeing partner is insolvent, all agree there is no guarantee, and she works. The reasoning for Abū Ḥanīfa is that the cause of appraisability (the child) is present but its effect on ownership is suspended for benefit; thus the cause operates to drop appraisability only after death. The text follows the order of the base text (sharḥ), expanding on the conditions and the ikhtilāf.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has given birth to a child by her master, and the child is acknowledged by him.
ضمان (ḍamān): Financial guarantee or compensation for the value of the other partner's share.
موسر (mūsir): Wealthy, having sufficient means to pay the guarantee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abū Ḥanīfa, what is required when a wealthy partner frees a jointly owned umm walad?
A) The slave woman must work for the other partner.
B) The freeing partner must pay a guarantee to the other partner.
C) No action is required because the umm walad is not appraisable.
D) The freeing partner must pay half the value to the other partner, but no guarantee.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute over the guarantee for freeing a joint umm walad hinges on whether her appraisability is suspended during the master's lifetime or only after death.

Death as Clarification in Ambiguous Manumission
Complex ruling on ambiguous manumission with death as clarification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on ambiguous manumission when a slave dies before the master's clarification.

Core Idea
When a slave dies, his death becomes the clarification—the ambiguity resolves automatically.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh deals with a master who makes two overlapping ambiguous statements to his slaves and then dies without clarifying his intent. The law then looks to events—specifically the death of one slave—to serve as a substitute for clarification. Understanding when and how death resolves ambiguity is essential for correct inheritance and freeing outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
There are three slaves: one who is initially present and later leaves (خارج), one who stays (ثابت), and one who enters (داخل). The master says 'one of you is free' first to the pair (outside and stayed), then after the exit and entry, to the pair (stayed and entered). He dies without clarifying.
The two statements create overlapping ambiguities.
2
Recognize the Default: Master Must Clarify
Normally, the master is required to clarify his intention. But if he dies before doing so, certain events step in as clarification.
Death of a slave is one such event.
3
Distinguish the Three Possible Deaths
The passage considers three possibilities: death of the one who left, death of the one who stayed, or death of the one who entered.
Each leads to a different outcome.
4
Apply the Rule for Each Death
If the one who left dies, the one who stays is freed by the first utterance, and the second utterance is void. If the one who stays dies, the one who left is freed by the first utterance and the one who entered by the second. If the one who enters dies, there is option in the first utterance.
The death removes one party, clarifying the ambiguity.
5
Note the Contingency for the Entrant's Death
If the entrant dies, the master's intention in the first utterance becomes crucial: if he meant the one who left (by the first), then the stayed is freed by the second; if he meant the stayed, then the second is void.
This introduces a dependence on the master's original intention, but since he died, the outcome is fixed according to the assumed intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العتق المبهم
(ولو قال لعبدين عنده من ثلاثة له : أحدكما حرّ، فخرج واحد ودخل آخر، فأعاد ومات بلا بيان عُتِق ممن ثبت ثلاثة أرباعه
[١] قوله : ولو قال.... الخ ؛ توضيح المسألة رجلٌ له عبيد ثلاثة فقال العبدين منهم وهما عنده في البيت أحدكما حرّ، فخرج واحد منهما من عنده ودخل الثالث، فقال المولى ثانياً مخاطباً للثالث والباقي من الأولين : أحدكما حرّ.
ففي هذه الصورة يؤمر المولى بالبيان، فإنّه هو المجمل، فإليه المرجع في بيان مراده في الإيجابين، فإن بدأ ببيان الإيجاب الأوّل، فإن قال عنيت الخارج عتق الخارج بالإيجاب الأول، وتبيّن أنّ الإيجاب الثاني بين الثابت والداخل وقع صحيحاً ؛ لوقوعه بين عبدين ، فيؤمر ببيان الإيجاب الثاني. وإن قال : عنيت الإيجاب الأول الثابت عتق الثابت به، وتبين أنَّ الإيجاب الثاني وقع لغواً ؛ لوقوعه بين حر وعبد ، و مثله لغو في ظاهر الرواية، كما لو قال لحر وعبد : أحدكما حرّ.
وإن بدأ ببيان الإيجاب الثاني فإن عنى به الداخلَ عتق الداخل بالإيجاب الثاني وبقي الأول بين الثابت والخارج على حاله، فيؤمر ببيانه. وإن عني به الثابت عتق الثابت به وعتق الخارج بالإيجاب الأول لتعينه للعتقِ بإعتاق الثابت.
وإن مات أحد العبيد قبل البيان يكون الموت بياناً ، فإن مات الخارج عتق الثابت بإيجاب الأول لزوال المزاحم، وبطل الإيجاب الثاني، وإن مات الثابت تعين الخارج بالإيجاب الأول والداخل وبالإيجاب الثاني، وإن مات الداخل خير في الإيجاب الأول، فإن عنى به الخارج تعيّن الثابت بالإيجاب الثاني، وإن عنى به الثابت بطل الإيجاب الثاني. كذا في «النهاية» و «فتح القدير» و«العناية» وغيرها.
(۱) «فتح القدير» (٤ : ٤٩١).
Chapter of Ambiguous Manumission
(And if he said to two slaves of his from three that he has: 'One of you is free', then one went out and another entered, and he repeated [the statement] and died without clarification, then of those who are established, three-fourths [of the total value?] are freed.
[1] His saying: 'And if he said...' etc.; clarification of the issue: A man has three slaves. He says to two of them who are with him in the house: 'One of you is free.' Then one of them goes out from him and the third enters, and the master says a second time, addressing the third and the remaining one of the first two: 'One of you is free.'
In this scenario, the master is ordered to clarify, for he is the one who made the ambiguous statement, so the reference is to him to clarify his intention in the two utterances. If he begins by clarifying the first utterance, then if he says 'I meant the one who went out,' then the one who went out becomes free by the first utterance, and it becomes apparent that the second utterance occurred between the one who stayed and the one who entered and is valid because it occurred between two slaves, so he is ordered to clarify the second utterance. And if he says 'I meant the first utterance for the one who stayed,' then the one who stayed becomes free by it, and it becomes apparent that the second utterance was void because it occurred between a freeman and a slave, and such is void in Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah, as if he said to a freeman and a slave: 'One of you is free.'
And if he begins by clarifying the second utterance, then if he intends by it the one who entered, the one who entered becomes free by the second utterance, and the first remains between the one who stayed and the one who went out in its state, so he is ordered to clarify it. And if he intends by it the one who stayed, the one who stays becomes free by it, and the one who went out becomes free by the first utterance because he is determined for freedom by the freeing of the one who stayed.
And if one of the slaves dies before clarification, then the death becomes clarification. If the one who went out dies, the one who stays becomes free by the first utterance due to the removal of the rival, and the second utterance becomes void. If the one who stays dies, then the one who went out is determined by the first utterance and the one who enters by the second utterance. If the one who enters dies, then there is option in the first utterance: if he meant by it the one who went out, then the one who stays is determined by the second utterance; and if he meant by it the one who stays, then the second utterance is void. Thus in al-Nihāyah, Fatḥ al-Qadīr, al-ʿInāyah, and others.
(1) Fatḥ al-Qadīr (4:491).
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement detailing the rules of ambiguous manumission (al-ʿitq al-mubham) in a complex scenario. The base text (matn) presents a case: a man has three slaves; he says to two of them 'one of you is free'; then one of them leaves and the third enters; he repeats the statement to the remaining one from the first two and the third; then he dies without clarifying his intent. The ruling given is that 'three-fourths of him is freed'—meaning that the total effect is that three-quarters of one slave is considered free? Actually the matn is ambiguous; the commentary explains that this is a shorthand. The commentary (sharḥ) then expands on the various possibilities: if the master clarifies before death, the outcome depends on his intention. The master is first asked to clarify. If he clarifies the first utterance, either meaning the one who left or the one who stayed. If he meant the one who left, then the second utterance is valid (between the stayed and the new entrant). If he meant the one who stayed, then the second utterance is void (since it becomes between a free man and a slave). If he clarifies the second utterance first, similar logic. If a slave dies before clarification, the death acts as a clarification: if the one who left dies, the one who stays is freed by the first, and the second is void; if the one who stays dies, the one who left is freed by first and the entrant by second; if the entrant dies, then there is ambiguity in the first utterance: if the master intended the one who left (by first), then the stayed is freed by second; if he intended the stayed, then second is void. The source references are given. The commentary is from al-Nihāyah, Fatḥ al-Qadīr, al-ʿInāyah. The passage ends with a footnote from Fatḥ al-Qadīr.

Key Terms
العتق المبهم (al-ʿitq al-mubham): Manumission where the master does not specify which slave is freed among a group, leaving it ambiguous.
الإيجاب (al-ījāb): The utterance of manumission; here it refers to each of the two statements 'one of you is free'.
لغو (laghw): Void or meaningless; an utterance that has no legal effect because it targets a free person mixed with a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The one who stayed is freed by the first utterance.
B) The one who entered is freed by the second utterance.
C) Both the stayed and the entered are freed.
D) None are freed until the master's intention is known.

Key Takeaway
Death of a slave in an ambiguous manumission scenario acts as a clarification, freeing the determined slave(s) according to precise rules based on which slave dies.

Ambiguous Manumission with Multiple Utterances and Death: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explaining the ruling on ambiguous manumission when the master dies without clarification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad regarding the share of the entering slave, and apply the proportional distribution rule to similar ambiguous manumission cases.

Core Idea
When a master dies after ambiguous utterances, freedom shares are proportional; the entering slave's share is half or quarter depending on the Imam.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah delves into a complex scenario of ambiguous manumission. A master makes two utterances involving three slaves, then dies without clarifying his intent. While the Hanafi school agrees on the shares for the slave who remained and the one who left, a sharp divergence emerges regarding the entering slave. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi scholars resolve overlapping ambiguous declarations when clarification becomes impossible, and highlights the principle that manumission can be partial when established by necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two utterances
First, understand the fact pattern: three slaves; first utterance to two present ('one of you is free'); one leaves, third enters; second utterance to the remaining one and the new entrant ('one of you is free'). The master dies without clarifying either utterance.
The passage first discusses the rule if the master clarifies, but the core dispute is when he dies without clarification.
2
Understand the default rule when the master clarifies
If the master clarifies, his stated intent is followed. The passage outlines various clarification scenarios to show that the utterances are valid and that clarification is always preferred.
This part is background; the main issue is death without clarification.
3
Focus on the case of death without clarification
When the master dies without clarification and all slaves are alive, the freedom shares are determined by proportional distribution. The first utterance is divided equally between the one who left and the one who remained; the second utterance is divided equally between the one who remained and the one who entered.
This proportional method is how ambiguity is resolved in Hanafi law.
4
Compare the positions of the two shaykhs and Imam Muhammad
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (two shaykhs) hold: one who remained (thabit) gets 3/4, one who left (kharij) gets 1/2, one who entered (dakhil) gets 1/2. Imam Muhammad agrees on the first two but gives the dakhil only 1/4.
The difference is only for the entering slave.
5
Analyze the reasoning and the indivisibility objection
The two shaykhs consider the second utterance as independently freeing half of each of its two targets. Imam Muhammad argues that half of the thabit is already freed by the first utterance, so the second utterance's half on the thabit is shared: only a quarter new manumission for thabit, and the other quarter goes to dakhil. The text also addresses an objection: the two shaykhs generally hold manumission is indivisible, but here fractional shares are allowed because they arise from distribution across states (tawzi‘ bi’i‘tibār al-ahwāl), not from a direct partial manumission of a known slave.
This reasoning shows the interplay of principles (qiyas vs. istihsan) and the concept of necessity.

Source Text

باب العتق المبهم
ولو قال لعبدين عنده من ثلاثة له : أحدكما حرّ، فخرج واحد ودخل آخر، فأعاد ومات بلا بيان عُتِق ممن ثبت ثلاثة أرباعه
[١] توضيح المسألة رجلٌ له عبيد ثلاثة فقال العبدين منهم وهما عنده في البيت أحدكما حرّ، فخرج واحد منهما من عنده ودخل الثالث، فقال المولى ثانياً مخاطباً للثالث والباقي من الأولين : أحدكما حرّ. ففي هذه الصورة يؤمر المولى بالبيان، فإنّه هو المجمل، فإليه المرجع في بيان مراده في الإيجابين، فإن بدأ ببيان الإيجاب الأوّل، فإن قال عنيت الخارج عتق الخارج بالإيجاب الأول، وتبيّن أنّ الإيجاب الثاني بين الثابت والداخل وقع صحيحاً ؛ لوقوعه بين عبدين ، فيؤمر ببيان الإيجاب الثاني. وإن قال : عنيت الإيجاب الأول الثابت عتق الثابت به، وتبين أنَّ الإيجاب الثاني وقع لغواً ؛ لوقوعه بين حر وعبد ، و مثله لغو في ظاهر الرواية، كما لو قال لحر وعبد : أحدكما حرّ. وإن بدأ ببيان الإيجاب الثاني فإن عنى به الداخلَ عتق الداخل بالإيجاب الثاني وبقي الأول بين الثابت والخارج على حاله، فيؤمر ببيانه. وإن عني به الثابت عتق الثابت به وعتق الخارج بالإيجاب الأول لتعينه للعتقِ بإعتاق الثابت. وإن مات أحد العبيد قبل البيان يكون الموت بياناً ، فإن مات الخارج عتق الثابت بإيجاب الأول لزوال المزاحم، وبطل الإيجاب الثاني، وإن مات الثابت تعين الخارج بالإيجاب الأول والداخل وبالإيجاب الثاني، وإن مات الداخل خير في الإيجاب الأول، فإن عنى به الخارج تعيّن الثابت بالإيجاب الثاني، وإن عنى به الثابت بطل الإيجاب الثاني. كذا في «النهاية» و «فتح القدير» و«العناية» وغيرها.
[٢] لأنَّ الإيجاب الأول ) دائر بين الخارج والثابت ، فينصفُ بينهما ، ثُمَّ الإيجابُ الثَّاني دائر بين الثَّابِتِ والداخل، فينصفُ بينهما ، فالنصفُ الذي أصابَ الثَّابت شاع فيه ، فما أصابَ النّصف الذي عُتِقَ بالإيجاب الأول لغا، وما أصاب النصف الفارغ، الربع بقي، فعتق من الثَّابِتِ ثلاثة أرباعه ، وأما من الداخل فيعتق ربعُهُ عند محمد الله ؛ لأن هذا الإيجاب لما أوجب عتق الربع من الثابت ، فكذا من الداخل؛ لأنه متنصف بينهما وإن مات المولى بلا بيان والعبيد أحياء عتق من الثابتِ ثلاثة أرباعه ومن الداخل نصفه، وكذا من الخارج، هذا عندهما. وعند محمد : من الداخل ربعه ومن الخارج نصفه ومن الثابت ثلاثة أرباعه. فإن قلت : قد مر أن العتق لا يتجزأ عندهما ، فينبغي أن تعتق الكل في هذه الصورة عندهما. قلت : عدم التجزء عندهما إنما هو إذا صادف الإيجاب محلاً معلوماً ، أما إذا ثبت بطريق التوزيع باعتبار الأحوال فيتجزأ ؛ لأنه ثابت ضرورة، والثابت بالضرورة يتقدّر بقدرها. كذا في «الكافي».
[١] قوله : ومن غيره ؛ أي يعتق من الثابت ثلاثة أرباعه، ومن الخارج نصفه كما قاله أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف .
[٢]قوله : لأن الإيجاب الأول... الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ الإيجاب الأول وهو قوله : أوّلاً : لعبدين عنده : أحدكما حرّ، دائر بين الثابت والخارج، فإذا لم يبين المراد منه ومات بلا بيان يقسم العتق بينهما بهذا الإيجاب، فيعتق به من كل من الثابت والخارج نصفه. والإيجاب الثاني: أي قوله أحدكما حرّ للثابت والداخل أيضاً، أوجب عتق نصف كل منهما ، إلا أنَّ نصف الثابت شاعَ في نصفيه ؛ لعدم التعيين، فما أصاب منه المستحق بالإيجاب الأول لغى لعدم المحلية، وما أصاب منه الفارغ من العتق عتق فتمت له ثلاثة أرباع ، وهذا بالاتفاق.
Chapter of Ambiguous Manumission. If a person says to two slaves from his three: 'One of you is free,' then one left and another entered, and he repeated it and died without clarification, then three-quarters of the one who remained is freed. Explanation of the issue: A man has three slaves; he says to two of them who are with him in the house: 'One of you is free.' Then one of them leaves and the third enters, and the master says a second time addressing the third and the remaining one of the first two: 'One of you is free.' In this situation, the master is ordered to clarify, for he is the one who made it ambiguous, so the reference is to him to clarify his intent in the two utterances. If he begins by clarifying the first utterance, if he says 'I meant the one who left,' then the one who left is freed by the first utterance, and it becomes clear that the second utterance between the one who remained and the one who entered occurred validly because it occurred between two slaves, so he is ordered to clarify the second utterance. If he says 'I meant the first utterance for the one who remained,' then the one who remained is freed by it, and it becomes clear that the second utterance was void because it occurred between a free man and a slave, and its like is void in the apparent narration, just as if he said to a free man and a slave: 'One of you is free.' If he begins by clarifying the second utterance, if he means by it the one who entered, then the one who entered is freed by the second utterance and the first remains between the one who remained and the one who left as it was, so he is ordered to clarify it. If he means by it the one who remained, then the one who remained is freed by it and the one who left is freed by the first utterance because he is determined for freedom by freeing the one who remained. If one of the slaves dies before clarification, the death is a clarification; if the one who left dies, the one who remained is freed by the first utterance due to the removal of the rival and the second utterance becomes void; if the one who remained dies, the one who left is determined by the first utterance and the one who entered by the second; if the one who entered dies, there is a choice in the first utterance: if he means by it the one who left, then the one who remained is determined by the second utterance; if he means by it the one who remained, then the second utterance becomes void. Thus in al-Nihaya, Fath al-Qadir, al-Inaya, etc. Because the first utterance is between the one who left and the one who remained, so it is divided half between them; then the second utterance is between the one who remained and the one who entered, so it is divided half between them. Then the half that hit the one who remained is shared in his halves; so what hit the half that was freed by the first utterance is void, and what hit the empty half remains, a quarter remains, so three-quarters of the one who remained is freed. As for the one who entered, his quarter is freed according to Muhammad, because this utterance when it necessitated freeing a quarter from the one who remained, similarly from the one who entered, because it is divided half between them. And if the master dies without clarification while the slaves are alive, three-quarters of the one who remained is freed, half of the one who entered, and likewise half of the one who left – this according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). According to Muhammad: from the one who entered a quarter, from the one who left half, and from the one who remained three-quarters. If it is said: It has been mentioned that manumission is not divisible according to the two, so all should be freed in this situation according to them. I say: Indivisibility according to them is only when the utterance hits a known subject; but if it is established by way of distribution considering the states, it is divisible because it is established by necessity, and what is established by necessity is limited by its measure. Thus in al-Kafi. [1] His saying: 'and from other than him,' i.e., three-quarters of the one who remained is freed and half of the one who left, as Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf said. [2] His saying: 'because the first utterance...' its summary: The first utterance, which is his statement first to two slaves with him: 'One of you is free,' is between the one who remained and the one who left; so if he does not clarify his intent and dies without clarification, manumission is divided between them by this utterance, so half of each of the one who remained and the one who left is freed. The second utterance, i.e., his statement 'one of you is free' to the one who remained and the one who entered, also necessitated freeing half of each; except that the half of the one who remained is shared in his halves due to lack of specification; so what hits from it the portion entitled by the first utterance is void due to lack of subject, and what hits from it the portion empty of manumission is freed, so he completes three-quarters, and this is by agreement.
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is an expansion probe on the chapter of ambiguous manumission (al-itiq al-mubham). It discusses a complex case: a master owns three slaves. He says to two of them who are present: 'One of you is free.' Then one of those two leaves, and the third enters. The master then says again, addressing the remaining slave from the first pair and the newcomer: 'One of you is free.' The master then dies without clarifying which slave he intended in each utterance. The passage first explains the procedure if the master were alive: he would be asked to clarify his intent. The details of different scenarios are given. Then it focuses on the case where the master dies without clarification and the slaves are still alive. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two shaykhs, al-Sahiban) rule that from the slave who remained (al-thabit), three-quarters is freed; from the one who left (al-kharij), half; and from the one who entered (al-dakhil), half. Imam Muhammad differs: he says from the one who entered, only a quarter is freed; from the one who left, half; and from the one who remained, three-quarters (same as the two). The reasoning is that the first utterance is between the one who left and the one who remained, so it frees half of each. The second utterance is between the one who remained and the one who entered, so it frees half of each. Thus the one who remained's half from the first utterance and half from the second utterance are combined, but since the half from the first utterance is already in his ownership, the second half overlaps. The distribution yields three-quarters for the one who remained. For the one who entered, the two shaykhs give him half (the second utterance alone), while Muhammad gives him only a quarter because the second utterance's effect is only half of that half? Actually, the text explains: According to Muhammad, the second utterance frees a quarter from the one who remained and a quarter from the one who entered, because the second utterance is divided between them, and since the one who remained already got half from the first utterance, the second utterance's half is shared, yielding a quarter for each. But the two shaykhs consider the second utterance as freeing half of the one who entered directly, ignoring the overlap? The text says: 'this according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). According to Muhammad: from the one who entered a quarter, from the one who left half, and from the one who remained three-quarters.' The commentary then addresses an objection: since the two shaykhs hold that manumission cannot be partial (la yatajazzā), why do they allow fractional shares here? The answer: indivisibility applies when the utterance directly addresses a known subject. Here, the shares are determined by distribution considering the states (bi-tariq al-tawzi‘ bi’i‘tibār al-ahwāl), so it is a necessity. The passage ends with a summary from al-Kāfī. This is a classic intra-Hanafi dispute where the underlying principles (qiyās vs. istihsān) lead to different distributions.

Key Terms
العتق المبهم (al-itiq al-mubham): A type of manumission where the master does not specify which slave is freed, e.g., saying 'one of you is free' to a group.
الإيجاب (al-ījāb): The utterance or statement of the master that effects manumission.
التنصيف (al-tanṣīf): The division of the freed share into halves between the possible recipients.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what fraction of the slave who entered (dakhil) is freed when the master dies without clarification?
A) One quarter
B) One half
C) Three quarters
D) The entire slave

Key Takeaway
In ambiguous manumission with multiple utterances and death, freedom shares are distributed proportionally; the entering slave gets half according to the two shaykhs and quarter according to Imam Muhammad.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Freedom Shares in Successive Ambiguous Manumission
Explains the calculation of freedom shares with two successive ambiguous statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the freedom shares of the internal slave (dakhil) in successive ambiguous manumission and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
The second ambiguous statement frees half of the internal slave according to Abu Hanifa, but only a quarter according to Muhammad—a dispute over how obstruction affects distribution.

This passage delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the calculation of freedom shares when a master makes two successive ambiguous statements. Understanding the reasoning of each side sharpens your ability to apply distributive logic in manumission cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the three slaves
The scenario involves three slaves: thābit (established, subject of both statements), khārij (external, subject only of first), and dākhil (internal, subject only of second). The master first says 'One of you is free' to thābit and khārij, then again to thābit and dākhil, then dies without specifying.
2
Apply the first assignment
The first statement distributes freedom equally between thābit and khārij: each gets half.
3
Apply the second assignment to thābit
The second statement aims to free half of thābit and half of dākhil. But thābit already has half freed from the first statement. The new half is shared across thābit's two halves: the portion overlapping the already freed half is void, so only an additional quarter is freed, making thābit three-quarters free.
This is agreed upon by all three imams.
4
Analyze the obstruction on thābit and apply to dākhil
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: The obstruction (from the first statement) only affects thābit, not dākhil. Therefore, the second statement frees a full half of dākhil. Muhammad: Since the second statement freed only a quarter from thābit due to obstruction, by analogy it should free only a quarter from dākhil.
5
Compare the results and identify thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf: thābit=3/4, khārij=1/2, dākhil=1/2. Under Muhammad: thābit=3/4, khārij=1/2, dākhil=1/4. The practical difference is the portion of dākhil that is free, affecting inheritance and slave value.

And from each of the others half, and according to Muhammad, from the one who enters a quarter, and from the other as they said. Because the first assignment revolves between the external and the established, so he halves between them. Then the second assignment revolves between the established and the internal, so he halves between them. The half that hit the established is shared among it: what hit the half that was freed by the first assignment is void, and what hit the empty half, the quarter remains, so three-quarters of the established are freed. As for the internal, one quarter is freed according to Muhammad; because when this assignment necessitated freeing a quarter of the established, likewise from the internal. [2] He said: Because the first assignment... etc. Its summary: The first assignment, which is his saying first to two slaves: 'One of you is free,' revolves between the established and the external. The second assignment, that is his saying 'One of you is free' to the established and the internal as well, necessitates freeing half of each, except that half of the established is shared in its two halves due to lack of specification; so what part of it was valid for the first assignment is void due to lack of [valid] locus, and what part of it was empty of freedom is freed, so it attains three-quarters, and this is by agreement.

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the calculation of freedom when a master makes two successive ambiguous statements. The scenario involves three slaves: one 'established' (thabit), one 'external' (kharij), and one 'internal' (dakhil). The master first says to two slaves: 'One of you is free,' which applies to the established and external. Then he says the same to the established and internal. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the first statement frees half of each of the established and external. The second statement is meant to free half of each of the established and internal, but the established already has half freed, so the half from the second statement is shared across the established's two halves; the portion overlapping the already freed half is void, so an additional quarter is freed (making three-quarters total). The internal has no prior freedom, so the second statement frees half of him. Muhammad disagrees: he argues that since the second statement freed only a quarter from the established (due to the obstruction), it should also free only a quarter from the internal. The commentator then answers a potential objection: if freedom is indivisible according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, why isn't the entire slave freed? The response is that indivisibility applies when the assignment targets a known person; here, freedom is established distributively based on states, so it is divisible by necessity.

Key Terms
ثابت (thābit): The slave who is the subject of both the first and second ambiguous statements (the 'established' one).
خارج (khārij): The slave who is only included in the first ambiguous statement (the 'external' one).
داخل (dākhil): The slave who is only included in the second ambiguous statement (the 'internal' one).
عتق مبهم (itq mubham): Manumission where the master does not specify which slave is freed, e.g., saying 'one of you is free.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One quarter
B) One half
C) Three quarters
D) The entire slave

Key Takeaway
In successive ambiguous manumission, the internal slave's freedom share differs between the majority (half) and Muhammad (quarter) due to differing views on the effect of obstruction.
