Testimony for Ambiguous Manumission: The Role of Tahrim al-Faraj
Ruling with proof explaining the distinction based on tahrim al-faraj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's ruling on testimony for ambiguous manumission and explain why the presence of tahrim al-faraj determines whether a claimant is required.

Core Idea
Testimony for ambiguous acts is accepted only when tahrim al-faraj is present, eliminating the need for a claimant.

Testimony for an ambiguous act – like manumitting one of two slaves – raises the question: must the claimant be specified? Abu Hanifa distinguishes between divorce and manumission based on a single factor: whether the act automatically prohibits intercourse (tahrim al-faraj). This lesson traces his reasoning and its application to male and female slaves.

Guided Path
1
State the contrasting rulings
Identify the two scenarios: testimony for divorce of one of two wives is accepted, but testimony for manumission of one of two male slaves is not. Write down the ruling for each.
2
Identify the key factor: tahrim al-faraj
Recognize that Abu Hanifa's distinction rests on whether the act inherently prohibits intercourse. Divorce always involves tahrim al-faraj, manumission of a male slave does not.
Tahrim al-faraj is considered a right of Allah, so no personal claimant is needed.
3
Apply to manumission of a female slave
Note that specific manumission of a female slave does involve tahrim al-faraj, thus testimony is accepted without a claimant. But ambiguous manumission (one of two) does not, so it is treated like the male slave case.
4
Understand the role of the claimant (da'wa)
When a claimant is required, it must be a specific individual. If the claimant is one of two unspecified persons, the claim is invalid, and the testimony fails.
5
Resolve the objection about volunteer testimony
The passage refutes an objection: even though manumission of a specific female slave is accepted as volunteer testimony (shahada hisbatan), the ambiguous case is not because it lacks tahrim al-faraj.
Volunteer testimony is only accepted for acts involving public rights like tahrim al-faraj.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما فرق أبو حنيفة له لأنَّ الدعوى شرط في عتق العبد عند أبي حنيفة له دون الطلاق؛ لأنَّ في الطلاق تحريم الفرج" ، وهو حق الله تعالى، فلا يشترط الدعوى، وفي العبد يشترط الدعوى، فإذا لم يكن المدعي ، وهو أحد العبدين) متعيناً لا يصح الدعوى.

وأما عتق الأمة فلا يشترط فيه الدعوى عند أبي حنيفة له إذا كان فيه تحريم الفرج ، أما إذا لم يكن فيشترط

وإن انعدام الدعوى إما في الطلاق لعدم الدعوى، لا يوجب خللا في الشهادة ؛ لأنها ليست بشرط فيها، ولو شَهِدَ أنه أعتق إحدى أمَتَيْه لا تقبل عند أبي حنيفة الله ، وإن لم يكن الدعوى شرطاً فيه ؛ لأنه إنما لا يشترط الدعوى لما أنه يتضمن تحريم الفرج فشابه الطلاق والعتق المبهم لا يوجب تحريم الفرج . عنده ، فصار كالشهادة على عتق أحد العبدين». انتهى

قوله : وإنما فرق ؛ أي بين الشهادة في عتق أحد العبدين وبين الشهادة في طلاق إحدى الزوجتين، حيث حكم بعدم قبول الأولى وبقبول الثانية. قوله : لأنّ في الطلاق تحريم الفرج ؛ الفرق بينه وبين العتق أنَّ الطلاق موضوع لثبوت حرمة الفرج في الحال أو في المآل كما في الطلاق الرجعي ؛ ولذا لا ينفك الطلاق عنه، بخلاف العتق ، فإنه ليس موضوعاً له ، ألا ترى إلى أنه ينفك عنه في عتق العبد ، وإنما يثبت هناك حرمة الفرج بتبعية زوالِ الرق أو زوال ملك الرقبة. قوله : وهو أحد العبدين ؛ أشار به إلى دفع ما يقال : إنه ينبغي قبول الشهادة إذا ادعى العبدان ذلك ؛ لوجود الدعوى، وحاصل الدفع : إن العتق الثابت إنما هو عتق أحدهما، فالمدعي في الحقيقة هو أحد العبدين؛ لأنه صاحب الحق، وهو غير متعين، فلم يوجد المدعي. قوله : إذا كان فيه تحريم الفرج ؛ وهو ما إذا كان العتق غير مبهم، فإنّ العتق متى وُجد في الأمة المعينة حَرُمَ الوطء بعده.

ففي عتق إحدى الأمتين لَغَتِ الشَّهادة، إذ ليس فيه تحريم الفرج عند أبي حنيفة ، فلا بد من الدعوى، فإذا لم يكن المدعي متعيّناً لم يصح الدعوى ، فلغت الشهادة

قوله : ففي عتق ...الخ ؛ بهذا يندفع ما يورد على أبي حنيفة له من أَنه لَمَّا كان مدار عدم قبول الشهادة في عتق أحد العبدين على أن العتق مما لا تقبل فيه الشهادة حسبة، كان ينبغي قبولها في عتق إحدى الأمتين ؛ لأنَّ عتق الأمة مما تقبلُ فيه الشهادة حسبة عنده. وملخص الدفع : إنَّ عتق الأمة إنّما تقبل فيه الشهادة حسبة ؛ لكونه متضمناً لتحريم الفرج ، فأشبه الطلاق، وهو مفقود في عتق إحدى الأمتين لحل وطئهما على رأيه ، فلذلك تلغو الشهادة عنده في هذه الصورة، وتشترط الدعوى.
Abu Hanifa distinguished because a claim is a condition for manumission of a slave according to Abu Hanifa, but not for divorce; because in divorce there is prohibition of intercourse, which is a right of Allah, so a claim is not required. In manumission of a slave, a claim is required, and if the claimant (one of the two slaves) is not specified, the claim is invalid. As for manumission of a female slave, a claim is not required according to Abu Hanifa if it involves prohibition of intercourse; but if it does not, then it is required. The absence of a claim in divorce does not cause a defect in testimony because it is not a condition in it. If one testifies that he manumitted one of his two female slaves, it is not accepted according to Abu Hanifa, even though a claim is not a condition in it, because the reason a claim is not required is that it involves prohibition of intercourse, making it similar to divorce. Ambiguous manumission does not cause prohibition of intercourse according to him, so it becomes like testimony on manumission of one of two slaves. End. He said: 'He distinguished' refers to the distinction between testimony on manumission of one of two slaves and testimony on divorce of one of two wives, where he ruled against acceptance of the former and acceptance of the latter. He said: 'Because in divorce there is prohibition of intercourse'; the difference between it and manumission is that divorce is established for the purpose of establishing the prohibition of intercourse immediately or eventually, as in revocable divorce, and thus divorce is never free from it. Unlike manumission, which is not established for that purpose; do you not see that it is free from it in manumission of a male slave? Rather, the prohibition of intercourse there is established as a consequence of the removal of slavery or ownership. He said: 'He is one of the two slaves'; this points to refuting what is said: 'Testimony should be accepted if the two slaves claim it, due to the existence of a claim.' The summary of the refutation is that the established manumission is only of one of them, so the claimant in reality is one of the two slaves, as he is the right-holder, and he is not specified, so the claimant does not exist. He said: 'If it involves prohibition of intercourse' refers to when the manumission is not ambiguous, for whenever manumission occurs in a specific female slave, intercourse becomes prohibited thereafter. So in the case of manumission of one of two female slaves, the testimony is void, because it does not involve prohibition of intercourse according to Abu Hanifa, so a claim is necessary, and if the claimant is not specified, the claim is invalid, so the testimony is void. He said: 'In the case of manumission...etc.'; by this, the objection against Abu Hanifa is refuted: that since the basis for non-acceptance of testimony in manumission of one of two slaves is that manumission is not accepted in testimony voluntarily, it should be accepted in manumission of one of two female slaves, because manumission of a female slave is accepted in testimony voluntarily according to him. The summary of the refutation: Manumission of a female slave is accepted in testimony voluntarily because it involves prohibition of intercourse, thus resembling divorce, and that is absent in manumission of one of two female slaves due to the permissibility of intercourse with both according to his view, so testimony is void in this case and a claim is required.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining Abu Hanifa's distinction between testimony for ambiguous manumission and ambiguous divorce. The commentator begins by stating the core ruling: Abu Hanifa differentiates because a claim (da'wa) is a condition for the validity of testimony in manumission of a male slave but not in divorce. The reason is that divorce intrinsically involves the prohibition of intercourse (tahrim al-faraj), which is a right of Allah, so no specific claimant is needed. In contrast, manumission of a male slave does not inherently involve this prohibition; therefore a claimant is required. If the claimant is one of two unspecified slaves, the claim fails due to lack of specification. The passage then addresses manumission of a female slave: if the manumission is specific (i.e., of a named female slave), it involves prohibition of intercourse, so no claim is needed. However, if it is ambiguous (one of two female slaves), it does not involve prohibition of intercourse because both remain permissible, so a claim is required, and without a specified claimant, the testimony is void. The commentator then responds to an objection: why not accept testimony for ambiguous manumission of a female slave if manumission of a female slave is generally acceptable as volunteer testimony? The answer: volunteer testimony is acceptable only when it involves prohibition of intercourse, which is absent in the ambiguous case. Hence, the testimony is void and a claimant is required.

Key Terms
العتق المبهم (al-'itq al-mubham): Manumission of one of two or more slaves without specifying which one.
تحريم الفرج (tahrim al-faraj): The prohibition of sexual intercourse, which occurs automatically in divorce and specific manumission of a female slave.
الشهادة حسبة (al-shahada hisbatan): Testimony given voluntarily without being a party or having a personal interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is testimony for ambiguous manumission of a male slave rejected while testimony for ambiguous divorce is accepted?
A) Because divorce involves prohibition of intercourse, a right of Allah, so no specific claimant is needed.
B) Because manumission requires two witnesses, while divorce requires only one.
C) Because the claimant in manumission is always the slave, who is unknown.
D) Because testimony for divorce is always accepted regardless of ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's ruling on testimony for ambiguous manumission hinges on tahrim al-faraj: present = no claim needed; absent = claim required, and ambiguity in the claimant invalidates testimony.

Why Testimony is Void in Freeing One of Two Female Slaves
Explaining the condition for voluntary testimony in ambiguous freeing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the condition of tahrīm al-faraj for accepting voluntary testimony in freeing female slaves and apply it to the case of ambiguous freeing.

Core Idea
Testimony in freeing a slave is voluntary only if intercourse is prohibited; absent that, a claimant is required.

In this passage, Abu Hanifa explains why testimony in the case of freeing one of two unnamed female slaves is void. While freeing a female slave normally accepts voluntary testimony (shahada hisba) because it resembles divorce in prohibiting intercourse, this specific situation lacks that element since both women remain permissible. Therefore, a specific claimant is needed, and without one, the testimony is invalid. The commentary also addresses an objection that might be raised against this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The passage states testimony is void in freeing one of two female slaves. Note the condition: lack of tahrīm al-faraj.
Tahrīm al-faraj means prohibition of intercourse.
2
Understand the principle of shahada hisba
Voluntary testimony is accepted in matters involving tahrīm al-faraj, such as divorce and freeing a female slave, due to public interest in lineage and modesty.
Shahada hisba requires no specific claimant.
3
Apply the principle to the case
In ambiguous freeing, the woman's status is not finalized, so no prohibition arises. Hence the analogy to divorce fails.
The absence of tahrīm al-faraj removes the basis for voluntary testimony.
4
Analyze the objection and rebuttal
The objection claims that since freeing a female slave normally accepts voluntary testimony, it should here. The rebuttal shows that the 'illa for acceptance is tahrīm al-faraj, which is absent.
This highlights the precision of Hanafi qiyas (analogy).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففي عتق إحدى الأمتين لَغَتِ الشَّهادة، إذ ليس فيه تحريم الفرج عند أبي حنيفة ، فلا بد من الدعوى، فإذا لم يكن المدعي متعيّناً لم يصح الدعوى ، فلغت الشهادة [ ١ ] قوله : ففي عتق ...الخ ؛ بهذا يندفع ما يورد على أبي حنيفة له من أَنه لَمَّا كان مدار عدم قبول الشهادة في عتق أحد العبدين على أن العتق مما لا تقبل فيه الشهادة حسبة، كان ينبغي قبولها في عتق إحدى الأمتين ؛ لأنَّ عتق الأمة مما تقبلُ فيه الشهادة حسبة عنده. وملخص الدفع : إنَّ عتق الأمة إنّما تقبل فيه الشهادة حسبة ؛ لكونه متضمناً لتحريم الفرج ، فأشبه الطلاق، وهو مفقود في عتق إحدى الأمتين لحل وطئهما على رأيه ، فلذلك تلغو الشهادة عنده في هذه الصورة، وتشترط الدعوى.
In freeing one of the two female slaves, the testimony becomes void, since there is no prohibition of intercourse according to Abu Hanifa, so a claim is necessary. When the claimant is not specific, the claim is not valid, so the testimony is void. [1] His saying: 'In freeing...etc.'; by this is repelled what is objected against Abu Hanifa that since the basis for non-acceptance of testimony in freeing one of two male slaves is that freeing is not a matter where testimony is accepted voluntarily, it should be accepted in freeing one of two female slaves, because freeing a female slave is a matter where testimony is accepted voluntarily according to him. The summary of the rebuttal: Freeing a female slave is accepted voluntarily because it entails prohibition of intercourse, so it resembles divorce, and this is absent in freeing one of two female slaves since both are permissible for him in his view, therefore testimony is void in this case, and a claim is required.
Source pages p. 663

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Abu Hanifa regarding the validity of testimony in a case of ambiguous freeing (ta'lif al-`itq) when one of two female slaves is freed. The matn states that testimony is void because there is no prohibition of intercourse (tahrim al-faraj) in this specific situation; since both women remain permissible for him until specification, the element that makes testimony in freeing a female slave acceptable (by analogy to divorce) is missing. Therefore, a specific claimant is necessary for the testimony to be valid. The commentary then addresses an objection: if testimony is not accepted in freeing one of two male slaves because freeing is not a matter for voluntary testimony (shahada hisba), then why is it also not accepted in freeing one of two female slaves, given that freeing a female slave normally does accept voluntary testimony? The response is that the acceptance in freeing a female slave is solely because it involves prohibition of intercourse, which is absent here. Hence, the analogy to freeing male slaves holds, and testimony is void. This highlights the principle that the `illa (effective cause) for accepting testimony in freeing female slaves is the prohibition of intercourse, and when that `illa is absent, the ruling reverts to the general rule.

Key Terms
تحريم الفرج (tahrīm al-faraj): Prohibition of intercourse; an effect of divorce or freeing a female slave that makes the woman unlawful to the man.
شهادة حسبة (shahāda ḥisba): Voluntary testimony given without a specific claimant, based on public interest or religious duty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the testimony lacks a specific claimant and the freeing does not involve prohibition of intercourse.
C) Because the testimony is given by a non-Muslim.
D) Because the freeing is considered a joke.

Key Takeaway
Testimony in freeing a female slave is accepted voluntarily only when it results in prohibition of intercourse; absent that, a specific claimant is required.

Conditional Freeing and Ownership at Fulfillment
Defining conditional freeing and its effect on slave ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define conditional freeing (al-`itq al-mu`allaq) and determine which slaves are freed when a condition is fulfilled, using the Hanafi criterion of ownership at the moment of fulfillment.

Core Idea
In conditional freeing, ownership at the moment of fulfillment—not at the oath—determines which slaves are freed.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on conditional freeing. The passage from 'Undat al-Ri'aya clarifies that when a master suspends freeing on a condition such as entry into a house, the critical factor is ownership at the time the condition is met, regardless of when the slave was acquired. This principle ensures that future acquisitions are also included, as long as they are owned at the exact moment of condition fulfillment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal nature
Conditional freeing (ta`līq al-`itq) is a valid cause that takes effect only upon the occurrence of the condition. It is called 'swearing by freeing' (al-halif bi al-`itq) and is distinct from unconditional freeing.
The author delayed this chapter because conditional freeing is weaker in causality.
2
Parse the example phrase
The matn gives: 'If I enter the house, then every slave I own on that day is free.' Understand that 'on that day' (yawmi'idh) refers to the time of entry, not the entire calendar day.
The entry (dakhul) is a momentary act, so 'day' means absolute time.
3
Determine the criterion
The relative pronoun 'man' (whoever) indicates that any slave owned at the moment of entry is freed, whether owned before or after the oath. The critical factor is ownership at fulfillment, not at the oath.
The commentary clarifies that 'yawmi'idh' is interpreted as 'the day when you entered'.
4
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
If the master buys a new slave after the oath but before entry, that slave is also freed upon entry because he is owned at that moment. The freeing applies to all slaves in his ownership at the time of condition fulfillment.
This includes slaves acquired after the oath, as long as they are owned at entry.
5
Note the linguistic interpretation
The word 'day' when connected to a non-extended act (like entering) is interpreted as absolute time, so even entry at night triggers the freeing. This interpretation is consistent with the divorce chapters.
This avoids the absurdity of requiring daytime entry if the condition is fulfilled at night.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحلف بالعتق ( ويعتق بإن دخلت الدّارَ فكل عبد لي يومئذ حرّ، مَن له حين دخل ملكه بعد حلفه أو قبله [1] قوله : باب الحلف بالعتق ؛ لما فرغ عن ذكـر العـتـق منجزاً شرع في العتق معلقاً ، وأَخَّرَه لكونه قاصراً في السببية، فإنّ العتق المنجز يكون سبباً في الحال، والمعلّقُ ينعقد سبباً عند وجودِ الشرط، والحلف - بفتح الحاء وكسرها – مصدر من . بالله ، وقد مَرَّ أنَّ الحلف يطلق على التعليق، فالحلف بالعتق أن يجعل العتق جزاءً لشرط بأن يُعَلِّقَ العتق بشيء. [٢] قوله : بأن دخلت الدار ؛ يحتمل أن يكون بصيغة التكلّم، فيكون العتق معلقاً على دخول المولى الدار، ويحتمل أن يكون بصيغة الخطاب خطاباً إلى أحد مماليكه ، أو إلى أجنبي، فيكون العتق معلقاً على دخول ذلك المخاطب الدار. [٣] قوله : مَن ؛ - بفتح الميم - موصولة ، وهو فاعل يعتق ؛ أي يعتق بالتعليق المذكور عبد مملوك له حين دخل الدار، سواءً كان مملوكاً عند التعليق أو لا، وذلك لأنَّ قوله يومئذ تقديره يوم إذ دخلت ، فدل ذلك على اعتبار قيام الملك وقت الدخول، فيعتق بذلك التعليق كلّ عبد كان مملوكاً له من حين الحلف إلى يوم الدخول، وكلّ عبد اشتراه بعد الحلف وهو في ملكه عند الدخول. [٤] قوله : حين دخل ؛ أشار به إلى لفظ : اليوم ، أريد به مطلق الوقت، فلو دخل ليلاً عتق أيضاً، وذلك لأنَّ اليوم إذا قرن بفعل لا يمتد يراد به مطلق الوقت ، وقد مرّ بحثه مع ما له وما عليه في أبحاث الطلاق. [0]قوله : ملکه ؛ بصيغة الماضي، وضمير الفاعل إلى المعلق ؛ أي قائـل ذلـك القول : أي سواءً كان ملك المولى ذلك العبد بعد تعليقه أو قبله ، وكذا لو ملك حين التعليق.
Chapter on Swearing by Freeing (and it takes effect if I enter the house, then every slave I own on that day is free, for him who owns at the time of entry, whether his ownership came after his oath or before). [1] His saying: Chapter on Swearing by Freeing; when he finished mentioning unconditional freeing, he began conditional freeing, and delayed it because it is lesser in causality, for unconditional freeing is a cause immediately, while conditional freeing becomes a cause at the occurrence of the condition. Al-halif (swearing) – with fath or kasr of the ha – is a verbal noun from 'I swore by Allah', and it has been mentioned that swearing is used for conditional language, so swearing by freeing means making freeing the consequence of a condition by suspending freeing on something. [2] His saying: 'if I enter the house' – it may be in the first person, so freeing is suspended on the master's entry, or it may be in the second person addressed to one of his slaves or to a stranger, so freeing is suspended on that person's entry. [3] His saying: 'man' – with fatha on the mim – is a relative pronoun, and it is the subject of 'is freed'; meaning, by the mentioned suspension, a slave of his ownership is freed at the time of entry, whether he was owned at the suspension or not, because his saying 'yawmi'idh' is interpreted as 'the day when you entered', indicating consideration of ownership at the time of entry. So by that suspension, every slave whom he owned from the time of the oath until the day of entry is freed, and every slave he bought after the oath while he is in his ownership at entry. [4] His saying: 'at the time of entry' – he pointed with it to the word 'day', meaning absolute time, so if he entered at night, it is also effective, because when 'day' is associated with a non-extended act, it means absolute time, and its discussion with its pros and cons has been covered in the chapters on divorce. [0] His saying: 'his ownership' – in the past tense, with the pronoun referring to the one who suspended; meaning, the speaker: that is, whether the master owned that slave after his suspension or before, and likewise if he owned at the time of suspension.
Source pages p. 664

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on swearing by freeing (al-hilf bi al-`itq), which deals with conditional freeing (ta`līq al-`itq). The author first notes the sequence: after discussing unconditional freeing, he now turns to conditional freeing, which is weaker in effect because it only takes effect upon fulfillment of the condition. The matn gives an example: 'if I enter the house, then every slave I own on that day is free.' The word 'man' (whoever) refers to any slave owned at the time of entry, regardless of whether the slave was acquired before or after the oath. The commentary clarifies that 'yawmi'idh' means the time of entry, even if at night, because 'day' here signifies a general time. The key point is that ownership at the moment of condition fulfillment is what matters; the slave is freed even if purchased after the oath, as long as he is owned at entry. The commentary also explains the possible interpretations of 'I entered' – whether the master or another person's entry. This chapter is foundational for understanding how conditional expressions in freeing operate in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
العتق المعلق (al-`itq al-mu`allaq): Conditional freeing; freeing that is suspended upon a future condition, taking effect only when the condition occurs.
يومئذ (yawmi'idh): Literally 'that day', but in this context interpreted as the time of entry, whether day or night.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example given (If I enter the house, then every slave I own on that day is free), which slaves are freed?
A) Only those slaves owned at the time of the oath.
B) Only those slaves owned at the time of entering the house.
C) Both those owned at the oath and those acquired later, as long as owned at entry.
D) Only male slaves.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, conditional freeing takes effect at the moment of condition fulfillment, and any slave owned at that moment is freed, regardless of when he was acquired.

Oath by Freeing Slaves with the Word 'Yawma'idh'
Ruling with commentary on 'yawma'idh' in oath by freeing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the word 'yawma'idh' refers to the time of entry, and determine which slaves are freed in a conditional freeing oath.

Core Idea
'Yawma'idh' points to the time of entry, not the oath—ownership at entry determines freedom.

This passage from the chapter on oaths by freeing slaves clarifies the interpretation of 'yawma'idh' (that day) in a conditional freeing. The Hanafi school holds that this phrase refers to the moment the condition is fulfilled, not the moment the oath is uttered. Understanding this linguistic point is crucial for correctly applying the ruling to cases where slaves are acquired after the oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath formulation
The example given is: 'If I enter the house, then every slave belonging to me on that day is free.' Note the use of 'yawma'idh'.
2
Locate the key phrase
'Yawma'idh' (that day) is the operative phrase. The commentary explains it refers to the time of entry.
3
Understand the commentary's interpretation
The commentator states that 'yawma'idh' is estimated as 'the day when you entered', indicating that ownership at the time of entry is the criterion.
4
Apply the rule
Apply this to determine which slaves are freed: all slaves owned at the moment of entry, regardless of when they were acquired (before or after the oath).
5
Note the implication for timing
The commentary also states that 'day' means absolute time, so entry at night also triggers the freeing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحلف بالعتق ويعتق بإن دخلت الدار فكل عبد لي يومئذ حر، من له حين دخل ملكه بعد حلفه أو قبله. قوله : باب الحلف بالعتق ؛ لما فرغ عن ذكر العتق منجزاً شرع في العتق معلقاً، وأخره لكونه قاصراً في السببية، فإن العتق المنجز يكون سبباً في الحال، والمعلق ينعقد سبباً عند وجود الشرط، والحلف - بفتح الحاء وكسرها – مصدر من حلفت بالله، وقد مر أن الحلف يطلق على التعليق، فالحلف بالعتق أن يجعل العتق جزاء لشرط بأن يعلق العتق بشيء. قوله : بأن دخلت الدار ؛ يحتمل أن يكون بصيغة التكلم، فيكون العتق معلقاً على دخول المولى الدار، ويحتمل أن يكون بصيغة الخطاب خطاباً إلى أحد مماليكه ، أو إلى أجنبي، فيكون العتق معلقاً على دخول ذلك المخاطب الدار. قوله : من ؛ - بفتح الميم - موصولة ، وهو فاعل يعتق ؛ أي يعتق بالتعليق المذكور عبد مملوك له حين دخل الدار، سواء كان مملوكاً عند التعليق أو لا، وذلك لأن قوله يومئذ تقديره يوم إذ دخلت ، فدل ذلك على اعتبار قيام الملك وقت الدخول، فيعتق بذلك التعليق كل عبد كان مملوكاً له من حين الحلف إلى يوم الدخول، وكل عبد اشتراه بعد الحلف وهو في ملكه عند الدخول. قوله : حين دخل ؛ أشار به إلى لفظ : اليوم ، أريد به مطلق الوقت، فلو دخل ليلاً عتق أيضاً، وذلك لأن اليوم إذا قرن بفعل لا يمتد يراد به مطلق الوقت، وقد مر بحثه مع ما له وما عليه في أبحاث الطلاق. قوله : ملکه ؛ بصيغة الماضي، وضمير الفاعل إلى المعلق ؛ أي قائل ذلك القول : أي سواء كان ملك المولى ذلك العبد بعد تعليقه أو قبله ، وكذا لو ملك حين التعليق.
Chapter of Oath by Freeing Slaves: And a slave is freed by [the statement] 'If I enter the house, then every slave belonging to me on that day is free' – whoever he owns at the time of entry, whether he owned him after his oath or before. His statement: 'Chapter of Oath by Freeing Slaves': When he finished mentioning immediate freeing, he began [discussing] conditional freeing, and delayed it because it is deficient in causation. Indeed, immediate freeing is a cause immediately, while conditional freeing becomes a cause upon occurrence of the condition. 'Al-hilf' (with fatḥa or kasra of the ḥā) is a verbal noun from 'I swore by Allah'. It has passed that 'al-hilf' is used for conditioning. So 'al-hilf bi al-'itq' is to make freeing a penalty for a condition by conditioning freeing on something. His statement: 'If I enter the house' – it is possible that it is in the first person form, so freeing is conditioned on the master's entering the house; and it is possible that it is in the second person form addressed to one of his slaves or to a stranger, so freeing is conditioned on that addressee's entering the house. His statement: 'Whoever' (with fatḥa of the mīm) is a relative pronoun and is the subject of 'is freed'; i.e., by the mentioned conditioning a slave whom he owns at the time of entering the house is freed, whether he was owned at the time of conditioning or not. That is because his statement 'on that day' is estimated as 'the day when you entered', so that indicates considering ownership at the time of entry. Thus by that conditioning, every slave that he owned from the time of the oath until the day of entry is freed, and every slave he bought after the oath and is in his ownership at the time of entry. His statement: 'When he entered' – he pointed by it to the word 'day', meaning absolute time. So if he entered at night, [the slave] is also freed, because when 'day' is connected with an action that does not extend, it means absolute time. Its discussion with its pros and cons has passed in the investigations of divorce. His statement: 'He owned' – in the past tense, and the pronoun of the subject refers to the one who made the conditioning; i.e., the speaker of that statement: i.e., whether the master owned that slave after his conditioning or before, and likewise if he owned at the time of conditioning.
Source pages p. 664

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter on oaths by freeing slaves. The author explains the rule when the oath includes the word 'yawma'idh' (that day). The matn states: if a person says 'If I enter the house, then every slave belonging to me on that day is free,' then any slave he owns at the moment of entry is freed, regardless of whether he owned that slave before or after making the oath. The commentary clarifies that 'yawma'idh' refers to the time of entry, not the time of the oath. Thus, slaves acquired after the oath but before entry are also covered. The commentary also explains that 'day' here means any time, so entering at night triggers the freeing. This ruling is built on the linguistic principle that 'yawma'idh' indicates a specific time linked to the action. The Hanafi school applies this condition strictly: the ownership must exist at entry, not at the oath.

Key Terms
يومئذ (yawma'idh): Refers to the time of the condition's fulfillment (entry), not the time of the oath.
معلق (mu'allaq): Conditional; a freeing that is contingent upon a future condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says 'If I enter the house, every slave belonging to me on that day is free,' and he then buys a slave after the oath but before entering, what happens when he enters?
A) That slave is freed because he owned him at entry.
B) That slave is not freed because he was not owned at the time of the oath.
C) The oath becomes invalid because the slave was not specified.
D) Only slaves owned at the time of the oath are freed.

Key Takeaway
The word 'yawma'idh' in a conditional freeing refers to the time the condition is fulfilled, not the time of the oath; ownership at that moment determines which slaves are freed.

Oath Without 'Yawma'idh' and the Fetus Exception
Ruling when oath lacks 'yawma'idh' and the exception for a fetus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on oaths lacking 'yawma'idh' to determine which slaves are covered and explain why a fetus is excluded based on the linguistic principle of present possession.

Core Idea
An oath covers only current slaves; a fetus is not included due to uncertainty of ownership at the moment of the oath.

This lesson examines a specific case in Hanafi fiqh: when a person makes an oath to free slaves but does not include the word 'yawma'idh' (that day). The ruling hinges on the linguistic interpretation of possessive phrases like 'my slave' as indicating present ownership. We also explore the exception for a fetus, which the Hanafi school excludes even if birth occurs within six months of the oath, due to the possibility that the pregnancy began after the oath. Understanding this rule clarifies how Hanafi jurists prioritize certainty and linguistic defaults in conditional oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase
The oath lacks 'yawma'idh'. The phrase 'every slave belonging to me' (كل عبد لي) is used. Determine what 'me' refers to in terms of time.
2
Analyze the linguistic basis
The possessive lām (لي) indicates current specification (اختصاص حالي). The derived noun 'mamlūk' also refers to present ownership. Understand why future ownership is not included.
The commentary explains the lām is for present possession.
3
Apply to the fetus case
If the owner says 'every male slave belonging to me is free,' the fetus is not considered a 'mamlūk' at the time of the oath because there is uncertainty (shubhah) whether it existed as a separate entity then.
Even if born within six months, the exact moment of conception is unknown.
4
State the Hanafi ruling
Only slaves owned at the exact moment of the oath are covered. The fetus is excluded due to lack of certainty of present ownership. This is the muftā bihī position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبلا يومئذ من له وقت حلفه فقط ، مثل : كل عبد لي أو أملكه حر بعد غد عنده لا الحمل بكل مملوك لي ذكر حر، وإن ولدته لأقل من نصف سنة. قوله : وبلا يومئذ ... الخ ؛ يعني لو لم يأت بلفظ يومئذ بل قال : إن دخلت الدار فكل عبد لي حر، يعتق من في ملكه حين حلفه فقط، ولا يعتق من اشتراه بعد الحلف، وبقى في ملكه إلى وقت الدخول ؛ وذلك لأن قوله : كل مملوك لي، وكل عبد لي ونحو ذلك إنما يشتمل من هو في ملكه في الحال، ولا يشتمل من يملكه بعد هذا القول. ومبناه على أن اللام للاختصاص، والمتبادر منه الاختصاص الحالي، ولا اختصاص في الحال بمن يملك بعده ، وأيضاً المشتق كالمملوك، وما في حكمه كالعبد حقيقة من قام به مبدأ الاشتقاق في الحال، على ما تقرر في موضعه. قوله : مثل...الخ ؛ الوجه فيه أن قوله : «لي» أو قوله: «أملكه» للحال، وهو الذي يتبادر منه لغة وعرفا وإنما يحمل على الاستقبال إذا انضمت إليه قرينة لفظية أو حالية، فيراد به من في ملكه حين الحلف، ويكون عتقه معلقاً على ما بعد الغد، فيعتق عند مجيء ما بعد الغد. قوله : لا الحمل...الخ ؛ يعني لو قال : كل مملوك لي ذكر فهو حر، وله جارية ذات حمل فولدت ذكراً لم يعتق ذلك الذكر، سواء ولدت بستة أشهر أو أكثر من وقت القول، أو ولدت لأقل. أما في الصورتين الأوليين فظاهر ؛ لأن لفظ المملوك للحال عرفاً ولغةً، وفي قيام الحمل وقت الحلف احتمال لوجود أقل مدة الحمل بعده ، فيحتمل أن تكون حملت بعد الحلف فلا يتناوله المملوك.
And without 'yawma'idh': only who he owns at the time of his oath [is freed], like: 'Every slave belonging to me or that I own is free after tomorrow' according to him – not the fetus: 'Every male slave belonging to me is free' – and if she gives birth to him in less than half a year. His statement: 'And without yawma'idh... etc.' – meaning if he did not come with the expression 'yawma'idh' but said: 'If I enter the house, every slave belonging to me is free,' only who is in his ownership at the time of his oath is freed, and not who he buys after the oath and remains in his ownership until the time of entry. That is because his statement 'every slave belonging to me' and 'every slave of mine' and the like only includes whoever is in his ownership at present, and does not include whom he owns after this statement. Its basis is that the lām is for specification, and what is immediately understood from it is current specification, and there is no current specification for whom he will own later. Also, the derived noun like 'mamlūk' and what is in its ruling like 'ʿabd' is, in reality, for one in whom the basis of derivation exists at present, as established in its place. His statement: 'like... etc.' – the point in it is that his saying 'belonging to me' or 'I own' is for the present, which is what is immediately understood linguistically and customarily. It is only carried to the future if a verbal or contextual indicator accompanies it. So it is intended to refer to whoever is in his ownership at the time of the oath, and his freeing is conditioned on after tomorrow, so he is freed upon the arrival of after tomorrow. His statement: 'not the fetus... etc.' – meaning if he said: 'Every male slave belonging to me is free,' and he has a pregnant slave girl who gives birth to a male, that male is not freed, whether she gives birth after six months or more from the time of the statement, or she gives birth in less. As for the first two cases, it is apparent because the word 'mamlūk' is for the present customarily and linguistically. And regarding the existence of the pregnancy at the time of the oath, there is a possibility that the minimum gestation period occurred after it, so it is possible that she became pregnant after the oath, thus the word 'mamlūk' does not include it.
Source pages p. 665

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on oaths by freeing slaves, now considering the case where the oath does not include the word 'yawma'idh'. The ruling is that only slaves owned at the very moment of the oath are included. The commentary explains that phrases like 'my slave' or 'what I own' are understood to refer to current ownership, not future acquisition. This is based on the linguistic principle that the possessive lām indicates present possession. The passage also gives an example: if someone says 'every slave I own is free after tomorrow,' only slaves owned at the time of the oath are freed after tomorrow; any slave acquired later is not included. Then the issue of the fetus is addressed: if a man says 'every male slave belonging to me is free,' and he has a pregnant slave who later gives birth to a male, that male is not freed. Even if the birth occurs within six months of the statement (which would suggest the pregnancy existed at the time of the oath), the fetus is not considered a 'mamlūk' at the time of the oath because there is uncertainty—it is possible the pregnancy began after the oath. Thus, the Hanafi school excludes the fetus from the ruling due to doubt.

Key Terms
الحمل (al-haml): The fetus in the womb.
أقل من نصف سنة (aqallu min nisfi sanah): Less than six months; the minimum period of gestation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says 'Every male slave belonging to me is free,' and he has a pregnant slave who gives birth to a male five months later, what is the ruling?
A) The male is freed because birth within six months shows pregnancy at the time of the oath.
B) The male is not freed because the word 'mamlūk' does not include the fetus due to uncertainty.
C) The oath is invalid because the fetus is unknown.
D) Only the mother is freed, not the child.

Key Takeaway
In oaths of freeing slaves, only those who are definitely owned at the time of the oath are covered; the fetus is excluded due to uncertainty, even if born within the minimum gestation period.

Tadbir and Wasiyyah: The Dual Declaration for Present and Future Slaves
Explains the dual nature of tadbir and wasiyyah in a declaration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a declaration of emancipation upon death that includes future slaves, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa (with Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A declaration freeing slaves upon death acts as tadbir for present slaves and as a will for future ones — but Abu Yusuf rejects the will aspect.

This passage from Sharh al-Wiqayah examines a master's declaration that 'every slave I own or will own is free after my death.' The commentator explains that this single statement has a dual nature: it is tadbir (conditional emancipation) for currently owned slaves, and a wasiyyah (bequest) for those acquired later. A key internal Hanafi ikhtilaf arises: Abu Yusuf restricts the effect to present ownership only, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad extend it to future acquisitions through the logic of bequests.

Guided Path
1
State the declaration and identify its two dimensions
The declaration 'every slave I own or will own is free after my death' contains both tadbir (attaching freedom to death) and wasiyyah (bequest after death). Recognize that the same words operate in two legal categories.
The classification is crucial because tadbir and wasiyyah have different rules regarding future ownership.
2
Understand the default ruling for currently owned slaves
Slaves owned at the time of the declaration become mudabbar: they cannot be sold or gifted, and will be freed upon the master's death (from the testamentary third if needed).
This part is uncontested among the Hanafi imams.
3
Identify the point of disagreement
The dispute focuses on slaves acquired after the declaration but before the master's death. Are they also freed upon death? If so, under what mechanism?
Abu Yusuf says no; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say yes.
4
Follow Abu Yusuf's argument
Abu Yusuf argues that the present tense 'I own' (أملكه) restricts the declaration to current ownership only. The later-acquired slave is not included in either tadbir or wasiyyah, so he remains a regular slave and is not freed upon the master's death.
His reasoning is based on the literal meaning of the wording.
5
Follow the majority Hanafi response with its qiyas
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad treat the declaration as a single act with a dual nature. The phrase 'after my death' (بعد موتي) signals a wasiyyah component. By analogy to bequests of property or to children (which include future acquisitions), the wasiyyah covers future slaves. Therefore, the later-acquired slave is freed from the testamentary third.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودبر بكل عبد لي أو أملكه حرّ بعد موتي من له يوم قال، لا من ملكه بعده

وإنما قيد بالذكر ؛ لأنه لو لم يقيد يعتق الحمل بتبعية الأم

ودبر بكل عبد لي أو أملكه حر بعد موتي من له يوم قال ، لا من ملكه بعده)، فقولهُ : مَن له يومَ قال : مفعول قوله : ودبر

وأما في الثالثة ؛ فلأنّ قيام الحمل وإن كان متيقناً عند الحلف هناك، لكن لفظ : «المملوك» يتناول المملوك المطلق ؛ أي الكامل والجنين مملوك تبعاً للأم لا مقصوداً، كيف لا فإنّه عضو من أعضاء الأم وجزءاً من أجزاءها ؛ ولهذا لا يملك بيعه منفرداً. كذا في «الهداية» وشروحها.

[١] قوله : وإنما قيد... الخ ؛ دفع دخل مقدر تقرير الدخل : إن لفظ المملوك لا يتناولُ الحمل مطلقاً، لما مرّ أنه يحمل على المطلق ؛ أي الكامل، والحمل ليس كذلك، سواء وصف المملوك بذكر أو لا ؛ فأي فائدة في تقييد المصنف له المملوك بالذكر. وتحرير الدفع : إنَّ عند عدم التقيد يختلفُ الحكم ، لا لأن الحمل يدخل في المملوك، بل لأنه لَمَّا لم يقيّد بالذكر دخلت به الجارية أيضاً ؛ لكون لفظ : المملوك ؛ بحسب الاستعمال شاملاً للذكر والأنثى كليهما ، وإذا أعتقت الجارية عتق حملها ذكراً كان أو أنثى تبعاً لها ؛ فلهذا قيد بالذكر.

[٢] قوله : بتبعية الأم ؛ ظاهره مخالف لما ذكره الشارح له قبيل باب عتق البعض، أنّ عتق الحمل بعتق أمه بطريق الأصالة لا بطريق التبعية، وقد مرا منا هناك بأنه يندفع التخالف فتذكره.

[۳] قوله : ودبر ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا قال : كلُّ عبد لي أو كل عبداً أملكه حرّ بعد موتي، يكون كلّ مَن في ملكه عند هذا القول مدبّراً ، لا مَن دخل في ملكه بعد حلفه وقبل موته لما مرّ أن قوله : كل عبد لي أو كلّ عبد أملكه لا يتناول إلا المملوك الحالي لا الاستقبالي ، فلا يدخلُ في إيجاب التدبير من يدخل في ملكه بعده.

(٤) قوله : مفعول قوله : ودبر ؛ ظاهره أن قوله : «ودير» في المتن ماض معروف من التدبير، والظاهر الأصوب أنّه على صيغة اسم المفعول، وقوله : «مَن له» مفعول ما ، ما لم يسمى فاعل الذي يقال له : نائب الفاعل، ويمكن أن يكون هو المراد من قول الشارح ه مفعول.

كتاب العتاق الحلف بالعتق والعتق على جعل

وإن مات عتقا الثلث. من

( إن مات عتقا من الثلث) (۱) ، اعلم أنه لما أضاف العتق إلى الموت ، فمن حيث إنه إيجاب العتق يتناول المملوك في الحال، فيصير مدبراً ؛ لتعليقه بالموت، فلا يجوز بيعه، ومن حيث إنه إيجاب بعد الموت ، يصير وصية

[١] قوله : وإن مات...الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ في الصورة المذكورة إذا مات المولى يعتق العبدان ؛ أي الذي كان في ملكه حين حلفه والذي دخل في ملكه بعد حلفـه وقـبل موته، لكن من الثلث كما هو حكم الوصيّة أنها تنفذ من الثلث لا مما زاد على الثلث ، فإن خرجا من الثلث أعتقا بحملها ، وإلا فيكون كل منهما معتق البعض.

وفيه خلاف أبي يوسف الله فإنّه يقول : لا يدخل العبد الذي دخل في ملكه بعد حلفه في الـعـتـق عـنـد مـوت المولى، كما لم يدخل في التدبير اتفاقاً ؛ لأنّ اللفظ حقيقة للحال، فلا يتناول ما ستملكه.

[٢] قوله : اعلم....الخ ؛ توجيه لقولهما من دخولهما في العتق ، وتوضيحه على ما في شروح «الهداية» أن قوله : كل عبد لي أو أملكه حرّ بعد موتي، إيجاب عتق من وإيصاء من وجه، أما كونه إيجاب عتق فظاهر، وأما كونه إيجاب إيصاء فلقوله : بعد موتي، وفي الوصايا تعتبر الحالة المنتظرة والحالة الموجودة حالاً كلاهما.

ألا ترى أنه لو أوصى بثلث ماله لرجل يدخل فيه المال الذي يملكه بعد هذا القول وقبل موته، ولو أوصى بشيء لأولاد فلان يدخل فيه من يولد له بعد الوصية وقبل موت الموصي أيضاً. فمن حيث أنه إيجاب عتق يتناولُ العبد المملوك حالاً فيصير مدبراً، ولا يتناولُ العبد الذي سيملكه فلا يصير مدبراً حتى يجوز بيعه ومن حيث أنه إيصاء يدخل فيه الذي سيملكه بعد هذا الإيجاب وقبل موته ، فكأنه قال عند الموت : كل مملوك أملكه حر، وهذا بخلاف قوله بعد غد في الصورة السابقة، فإنّه تصرّف واحد، وهو إيجاب العتق وليس هناك إيصاء.

(۱) أي عتق الأول بسبب التدبير وعتق الثاني بسبب إضافة العتق إلى الموت. ينظر: «مجمع الأنهر (۱ : ٥٢٧).
He declared as mudabbar every male slave I have or will own free after my death, [but] only those [owned] on the day he spoke, not those he owns afterwards. He restricted it to male because if he did not restrict it, the fetus would be freed by following the mother. The phrase 'every male slave I have or will own free after my death' makes those present at the time mudabbar, not those acquired later, because the words only apply to current possession. If he dies, they are freed from the third [of the estate]. He said: Know that when he attaches freedom to death, from the aspect of effecting freedom it applies to current slaves, making them mudabbar, so they cannot be sold; from the aspect of being after death, it becomes a will. Thus, upon death both slaves are freed (the one owned at the oath and the one acquired after) but from the third, as is the rule for wills. Abu Yusuf disagrees, saying the later-acquired slave is not freed because the wording is for the present. The justification for their view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that this statement is both an effecting of freedom and a bequest. The bequest aspect considers future and present states, as seen in analogies of bequests of property or for children.
Source pages p. 666

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Wiqayah discusses the ruling and internal Hanafi disagreement on the declaration: 'Every slave I own or will own is free after my death.' The commentator explains that the restriction to male slaves (بكل عبد) is to avoid the consequence that if a female slave (جارية) were included, freeing her would automatically free her unborn child by following, which the master may not intend. The main issue is whether 'will own' includes future acquisitions. The text states that according to the relied-upon position (that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani), the declaration operates in two ways: (1) as tadbir (emancipation upon death) for currently owned slaves, making them mudabbar and unsaleable; (2) as a will (wasiyyah) for slaves acquired after the declaration but before death, so they are freed upon death but from the testamentary third (ثلث) of the estate. Abu Yusuf contends that the latter slave is not freed at all, because the expression 'I own' (أملكه) is in the present tense and cannot apply to future ownership. The Hanafi response uses qiyas (analogy) to the rule of bequests: if one bequeaths one-third of his property, it includes property acquired after the will; similarly, if one bequeaths something to someone's children, it includes children born after the will. Therefore, the declaration 'every slave I will own' encompasses future slaves by virtue of being a will. This differs from the earlier case of 'after tomorrow' (بعد غد) which is a single act of tadbir only, not a will. The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the later-acquired slave is freed from the third; under Abu Yusuf, it is not freed at all, possibly forcing a partial manumission.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): A declaration by a master that a slave will be free upon the master's death, making the slave a mudabbar.
وصية (wasiyyah): A bequest or will that takes effect after death, limited to one-third of the estate.
ثلث (thuluth): One-third of the estate, the maximum portion that can be allocated through a will without heirs' consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if a master says 'Every slave I own or will own is free after my death' and later acquires a new slave before dying, what happens to that new slave upon the master's death?
A) The slave is freed unconditionally from the entire estate.
B) The slave is freed from the testamentary third as part of a will (wasiyyah).
C) The slave is not freed at all because the expression 'I own' is present tense.
D) The slave becomes mudabbar and cannot be sold.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a declaration freeing slaves upon death is both tadbir and wasiyyah, covering future slaves from the testamentary third.

Rulings on Freeing a Slave for Payment
States the ruling and conditions for freeing a slave for a fixed payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid emancipation-for-fee contract, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding partial acceptance, and distinguish the debt from kitabah for suretyship purposes.

Core Idea
Emancipation for payment frees the slave immediately, turning the payment into a valid debt.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on al-‘itq ‘ala ja‘l, where a master frees his slave in exchange for a specified payment. The passage clarifies the conditions for the contract’s validity, the effect of the slave’s acceptance, and the legal nature of the resulting debt. It also highlights a key dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions over partial acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
Read the matn: the slave is freed immediately upon acceptance, and the payment becomes a debt. Note the contrast with kitabah.
The debt is considered 'dayn sahih' (valid debt) for suretyship.
2
Analyze the conditions of offer and acceptance
Understand that the master's offer can use 'for' or 'with', but context shows immediate intention. Acceptance must be of the full amount in the same session.
Failure to accept fully or rejection voids the contract.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Learn Imam Abu Hanifa's view that partial acceptance does not free the slave, and the Sahibayn's view that it frees entirely with full payment due.
This affects the slave's status and debt obligation.
4
Understand the debt’s validity for kafalah
The debt is binding and cannot be dropped by incapacity, unlike kitabah debt, making it suitable for suretyship.
Kitabah debt falls if the slave becomes unable to pay.

بخلاف
Chapter of Emancipation for a Fee
And whoever is freed for a property or with it and he accepts, he is freed, and the property is a debt upon him, and suretyship is valid for it, unlike the consideration of kitabah. Its form is that he says: 'You are free for a thousand' or 'with a thousand' and he accepts, he is freed and the property is a debt upon him. Suretyship for it is valid because it is a valid debt, being a debt upon a free person, unlike the consideration of kitabah, which is a debt upon his slave.
[1] His statement: 'or with it.' If you say: his immediate freedom in the case of 'with' is apparent, but in the case of 'for' it is not apparent because 'for' indicates a condition, making freedom conditional upon payment of the property, so he is not freed immediately. I say: The discussion here is when his intention is immediate liberation in exchange for compensation, not suspension; so the contextual indication diverts it from conditionality. Thus in al-‘Inayah. [2] His statement: 'and he accepts' meaning all of the property in the session. If he does not accept all of the property or some of it, he is not freed; because it is a transaction on the slave's side, so his acceptance is required in that session just as in other transactions. If he rejects or turns away by leaving the session or engaging in another act that terminates the session, he is not freed. If he accepts some of the property, he is not freed according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) because it causes harm to the master. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it is valid and he is freed entirely. Thus in al-Bahr and al-Nahr. [3] His statement: 'and the property is a debt upon him' meaning it is obligatory for him to pay it to the master after his freedom, unlike the mukatab, who does not become free without payment and is not freed by mere acceptance. [4] His statement: 'because it is a valid debt' has been established in its place: suretyship is only valid for a valid debt, which is something that does not fall from the debtor except by payment or by release from the right-holder. And the consideration of kitabah, i.e., the property for which he has made a kitabah contract with his slave, is not a valid debt because it falls without payment or release as well, such as when the slave renders himself incapable (by inability to pay). Unlike
Source pages p. 669

Commentary
This is a new chapter on emancipation for a fee (al-‘itq ‘ala ja‘l). The matn states the basic ruling: if a master says to his slave 'you are free for a thousand' or 'with a thousand' and the slave accepts, the slave is freed immediately and the thousand becomes a debt upon him. This debt is valid and thus suretyship (kafalah) is permissible for it, unlike the case of kitabah (contract of manumission by payment) where the debt is upon the slave and not valid for kafalah. The commentary clarifies conditions: (1) The word 'with' (bi) indicates immediate freedom, while 'for' (‘ala) can imply a condition; but the context (qarinat al-hal) shows the intention is immediate liberation with a fee, so 'for' is interpreted as immediate as well. (2) Acceptance must be for the entire amount in the same session; partial acceptance or rejection voids the contract. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa says partial acceptance does not free the slave because it harms the master, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) say it is valid and the slave is freed for the whole amount. (3) After freedom, the debt is obligatory upon the freed slave; this differs from mukatab who remains a slave until payment. (4) The reason kafalah is valid is that the debt is considered a 'valid debt' (dayn sahih) which does not fall except by payment or forgiveness, unlike the debt of kitabah which falls when the slave becomes unable to pay.

Key Terms
العتق على جعل (al-'itq 'ala ja'l): A contract where the master frees his slave immediately in exchange for a specified payment (mal) that becomes a debt upon the freed slave.
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitabah): The consideration in a kitabah contract, where the master agrees to free the slave upon payment of installments, but the slave remains a slave until full payment.
دين صحيح (dayn sahih): A valid debt that cannot be dropped except by payment or formal release (ibra) by the creditor. Used here to contrast with kitabah debt which can drop due to incapacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if a slave accepts only part of the compensation in an emancipation-for-fee contract?
A) The slave is freed and must pay only what he accepted.
B) The slave is not freed according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
C) The slave is freed and must pay the full amount according to all Hanafi imams.
D) The contract becomes kitabah automatically.

Key Takeaway
Emancipation for a fee frees the slave immediately, with the payment becoming a binding debt that is valid for suretyship, unlike kitabah.

The Suspended-Emancipation Slave: Definition and Seven Differences
Definition and enumeration of seven differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of the suspended-emancipation slave and list the seven Hanafi differences that distinguish him from the mukatab.

Core Idea
A slave whose freedom depends on his own payment is not a mukatab – no acceptance needed, cannot reject, and third-party payment does not free him.

The Hanafi fiqh of emancipation includes a specific category: a slave whose master says, 'If you pay me X, you are free.' This slave is not a mukatab (contract slave) but a 'mu'allaq 'itquhu bil-ada'. He remains a slave until he pays, yet he gains implicit permission to trade. Understanding the seven differences between these two categories is essential for correctly applying rulings of sale, debt, and inheritance. This lesson will dissect those differences as laid out in the classical Hanafi texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage contrasts two types of slaves seeking freedom: the one whose emancipation is suspended on payment (المعلق عتقه بالأداء) and the mukatab (المكاتب). Notice that both aim for freedom through payment, but the legal structure differs.
Both are distinct from immediate emancipation for a price (العتق على جعل).
2
List the seven differences
The text enumerates seven points where the rulings diverge. Work through each carefully: (1) acceptance needed? (2) can the slave reject? (3) can the slave be sold? (4) effect of third-party payment? (5) payment after master's death? (6) session restriction for 'if'? (7) status upon death with sufficient funds?
Use the text's order; each difference has a clear practical consequence.
3
Understand the rationale behind each difference
For each difference, ask: why does the Hanafi school rule this way? For example, the suspended-emancipation slave is not freed by third-party payment because the condition is his own act (أداؤه المال إلى المولى), unlike the mukatab where the contract can be performed by another.
This reveals how conditions (shurut) shape the ruling.
4
Apply the differences to a scenario
Given a case where a master says, 'If you pay me 1000 dirhams, you are free,' and the slave says 'I refuse,' test difference #2: the suspension remains valid. Then test #4: a friend pays on his behalf – he is not freed.
This solidifies the distinction.

(۱) «الدر المختار شرح تنوير الأبصار»(٣: ٦٧٦).

كتاب العتاق الحلف بالعتق والعتق على جعل
مأذون إن أدى عُتِقَ لا مكاتب، ويقيّد أداؤه بالمجلس إن عَلَّقَ : بإن، وبإذا لا ، ورجع المولى عليه إن أدى مما كسبه قبل التعليق لا مما بعده، وعتق في حاليه ، وإن خَلَّى بينه وبينه
مأذون إن أدى عُتِق لا مكاتب ) (۱) : صورتُهُ أن يقول : إن أديت إليَّ كذا ، فأنتَ حرّ، فإنه يصير مأذونا بالتجارة ؛ ليتمكن من أداء المال ، ويقيد أداؤه بالمجلس إن عَلَّقَ : بإن ، وبإذا لا ) (۲) : أي لا يقيد بالمجلس ، ورجع المولى عليه إن أدى مما كسبه قبل التعليق لا مما بعده ، وعُتِق في حاليه ) : أي في حال أدائِهِ مما كسبه قبل التعليق ، وحال أدائه مما كسبه بعده ، وإن خَلى بينه وبينه ) : أي بين المولى وبين المال [١] قوله : مأذون ؛ لأنّ رغبته في الاكتساب بطلب المولى الأداء منه، فيكون إذناً بالتجارة دلالة، لكنّه يتقيّد بالمجلس في صورة التعليق بإن
[٢]قوله : ويقيد ؛ يعني إن علق عتقه على أداء المال بكلمة : «إن»، يشترط أن يكون أداؤه في ذلك المجلس، فيعتق بأدائه فيه، لا بأدائه بعده ، وإن علقه بكلمة : «إذا» أو بكلمة : متى» لا يتقيد بالمجلس ؛ لأنها لعموم الأوقات، وقد مرّ بحث ذلك في أبحاث تعليق الطلاق. [٣] قوله : ورجع ؛ يعني إن أدّى العبد من المال الذي كسبه قبل التعليق رجع
و
المولى عليه ؛ لأنَّ ذلك كلّه للمولى، لا من إكسابه بعد التعليق. [٤]قوله : وعتق في حاليه ؛ يعني يصيرُ العبد معتقاً بأداء المال الذي علقه به ، سواءً أداه من إكسابه السابقة أو من إكسابه اللاحقة ؛ لوجود الشرط، وهو أداء المال
المفروض. [0] قوله : وإن خلى...الخ ؛ الواو وصليّة، وإنّما أتى بذلك ليدل على أنه يحصل الأداء بالقبض الحقيقي بالطريق الأولى، ووجه التنصيص على التخلية دفع توهم عسى أن يتوهم أن المعلق عتقه بالأداء لا يعتق إلا إذا وجد الأداء الحقيقي، وذا لا يحصل
بالتخلية.
(۱) لأنه صريح في تعليق العتق بالأداء وإنما صار مأذوناً ؛ لأن المولى رغبه في الاكتساب بطلبه الأداء منه ، ومراده التجارة لا التكدي فكان إذناً له دلالة، فجاز بيعه، ولا يكون العبد أحق بمكاسبه
حتى جاز للمولى أخذا منه بلا رضاء بخلاف المكاتب. وتمامه في درر الحكام (٢ : ١٥). (٢) لأنه لا يستعمل للوقت كمتي.
And the one whose emancipation is suspended upon payment... He remains a slave as before, so guaranteeing him is not valid, unlike the property by which he is freed immediately or for which he is liable, for the slave becomes free immediately upon acceptance without waiting for payment, and the property becomes a debt upon him because it is a substitute for his emancipation, and it is not dropped except by payment or forgiveness, so it is a valid debt and guaranteeing it is valid. (1) His statement: 'and the suspended' – 'al' is a relative pronoun, and 'his emancipation' in nominative is the subject; meaning the one who suspended his emancipation upon payment of money is a permitted trader, not free immediately, but after paying the money. The difference between him and the mukatab, as detailed in 'Tanwir al-Absar' and its commentaries, is in several aspects: 1. The suspended-emancipation slave's emancipation does not depend on acceptance, unlike the mukatab, whose emancipation depends on acceptance. 2. It is not invalidated by his rejection, like the slave saying 'I am not pleased with it', unlike the mukatab, whose kitabah is invalidated by rejection. 3. He may be sold before the condition occurs, unlike the mukatab, who may not be sold except with his consent. 4. If someone else pays the money on his behalf voluntarily, or he orders another to pay and he pays, he is not freed; because the condition is his own payment to the master, unlike the mukatab, who is freed by that. 5. If the master dies and the slave pays the money to his heirs, he is not freed due to the loss of the condition, unlike the mukatab. 6. It is required that he pay in the session if he suspended his emancipation with the word 'if', but not so for the mukatab. 7. If he dies and leaves money equal to the amount on which his emancipation was suspended, he is not ruled to be free, unlike the mukatab, who if he dies leaving sufficient payment, is ruled to be free in the last part of his life. There are also other aspects of difference detailed in the comprehensive works. And with this explanation, the difference between the one whose emancipation is suspended on payment and the one freed immediately for or with money becomes clear. (1) 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar Sharh Tanwir al-Absar' (3:676).

Book of Emancipation: Swearing by emancipation and emancipation for a fee. A permitted trader: if he pays he is freed, not a mukatab. His payment is restricted to the session if he suspends with 'if', but with 'when' not. The master may take from him what he earned before the suspension, not after. He is freed in both cases. And if he leaves him free with the property... 'A permitted trader if he pays he is freed, not a mukatab': its form is that he says 'If you pay me such-and-such, you are free', then he becomes a permitted trader to enable him to pay the money. His payment is restricted to the session if he suspends with 'if', but with 'when' not, meaning it is not restricted to the session. The master may take from him what he earned before the suspension, not after. He is freed in both cases: meaning in the case of payment from what he earned before the suspension and in the case of payment from what he earned after. 'And if he leaves him free with the property': meaning between the master and the property. [1] His statement: 'a permitted trader' because his desire to earn arises from the master's demand for payment, so it is an implicit permission to trade, but it is restricted to the session in the case of suspension with 'if'. [2] His statement: 'is restricted' means if he suspended his emancipation on payment with the word 'if', it is required that he pay in that session, so he is freed by paying in it, not by paying after, but if he suspended with 'when' or 'whenever', it is not restricted to the session because they indicate general times. This discussion has already passed in the discussions of suspending divorce. [3] His statement: 'the master may take' means if the slave pays from money he earned before the suspension, the master may take it from him, because all that belongs to the master, not from his earnings after the suspension. [4] His statement: 'freed in both cases' means the slave becomes free upon paying the money on which his emancipation was suspended, whether he pays from his previous earnings or from his subsequent earnings, because the condition (paying the prescribed money) exists. [0] His statement: 'and if he leaves free...' the 'waw' is conjunctive. He brought this to indicate that payment occurs through actual taking in the primary sense, and the specification of leaving free is to dispel the misconception that the suspended-emancipation slave is only freed if actual payment occurs, which does not happen by leaving free. (1) Because it is explicit in suspending emancipation on payment, and he becomes a permitted trader only because the master encouraged him to earn by demanding payment from him, and his intention is trade, not begging, so it is an implicit permission. Thus his sale is permissible, and the slave is not entitled to his earnings, so the master may take from him without his consent, unlike the mukatab. Its completion is in 'Durar al-Hukkam' (2:15). (2) Because it is not used for a specific time like 'whenever'.
Source pages p. 670

Commentary
This passage defines the legal category of a slave whose emancipation is suspended upon payment (المعلق عتقه بالأداء). The text is from the matn of the Book of Emancipation, with commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. It begins by distinguishing this slave from one freed immediately for a price. In immediate emancipation, the slave becomes free instantly upon acceptance, and the price becomes a debt. But here, the slave remains a slave until he pays. The passage then lists seven differences between this slave and a mukatab (who contracts for his freedom): (1) No acceptance needed; (2) Cannot be invalidated by the slave's rejection; (3) Can be sold before the condition occurs; (4) Payment by a third party does not free him; (5) Payment to heirs after master's death does not free him; (6) If suspended with 'إن' (if), payment must be in the same session; (7) If he dies with enough money, he is not deemed free, unlike a mukatab. The commentary then explains the key rule: the slave becomes a permitted trader (مأذون) by implication to enable earning. If the suspension is with 'إذا' (when), payment is not restricted to the session. The master can reclaim earnings made before the suspension but not after. The slave is freed whether he pays from pre- or post-suspension earnings. The final point clarifies that if the master leaves the slave free with the property (i.e., lets him use it), it does not constitute actual payment; actual delivery is required. This passage is crucial for understanding the nuanced Hanafi position on suspended emancipation and its practical distinctions from kitabah and immediate emancipation.

Key Terms
المعلق عتقه بالأداء (al-mu'allaq 'itquhu bil-ada'): A slave whose master has declared that he will be freed upon payment of a specified sum. The slave is not freed until he pays, but he is treated as a permitted trader (مأذون) by implication.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has entered into a formal contract (kitabah) with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments. The contract requires acceptance and can be invalidated by the slave's rejection.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave whom the master has permitted to engage in trade. Here, the master's demand for payment implies permission to trade, making the slave a مأذون دلالة (implicitly permitted).
تعليق العتق (ta'liq al-'itq): Suspending emancipation upon a future condition, such as payment. The freedom takes effect only when the condition is fulfilled.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a difference between the suspended-emancipation slave and the mukatab?
A) The suspended-emancipation slave must accept the condition, while the mukatab does not.
B) The suspended-emancipation slave can be sold before the condition occurs, while the mukatab cannot be sold without his consent.
C) If the slave dies with enough money, the suspended-emancipation slave is deemed free, unlike the mukatab.
D) Third-party payment frees both types of slaves.

Key Takeaway
The suspended-emancipation slave is a distinct Hanafi category with seven specific differences from the mukatab, fundamentally differing in the role of acceptance, rejection, third-party payment, and sale.

Conditional Manumission: Implied Permission and Constructive Delivery
Explains rulings of slave freed conditionally on payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of conditional manumission upon payment and explain how the choice of particle ('in' vs 'idha') affects the time restriction, and how constructive delivery (takhliya) suffices for fulfillment.

Core Idea
The condition's exact wording determines the payment deadline; constructive delivery is as valid as physical possession.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the Hanafi ruling on a master's conditional promise of freedom in exchange for payment. The slave automatically becomes a ma'dhun (permitted to trade) to enable earning. The lesson clarifies the key role of the conditional particle, the validity of constructive delivery, and the distinction between this case and the kitaba contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is conditional manumission upon payment. The ruling is that it is valid (sahih). The slave becomes a ma'dhun by implication.
The master's demand for payment implies permission to trade.
2
Examine the particle effect
If the condition uses 'in' (إن), payment must occur in the same assembly. If 'idha' (إذا) or 'mata' (متى), no time restriction.
'In' is for immediacy; 'idha' indicates generality of time.
3
Understand constructive delivery (takhliya)
Takhliya means removing obstacles and placing money within the master's reach. This constitutes valid delivery; the slave is freed even without physical possession by the master.
This is stated a fortiori: if takhliya suffices, actual possession certainly does.
4
Recognize the partial payment and type mismatch rules
The condition requires full payment of the exact thing specified. Paying part, a different type (e.g., dinars instead of dirhams), or at a different time voids the condition.
Unlike mukatib, reduction (hatt) by the master does not free the slave unless full payment is made.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on mukatib
According to the general Hanafi view, a mukatib is also freed by takhliya. Zufar holds that the mukatib requires actual possession.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.

[٣] قوله : ورجع ؛ يعني إن أدّى العبد من المال الذي كسبه قبل التعليق رجع

المولى عليه ؛ لأنَّ ذلك كلّه للمولى، لا من إكسابه بعد التعليق. [٤]قوله : وعتق في حاليه ؛ يعني يصيرُ العبد معتقاً بأداء المال الذي علقه به ، سواءً أداه من إكسابه السابقة أو من إكسابه اللاحقة ؛ لوجود الشرط، وهو أداء المال
